What estimator was used? What model or sampling approximation justifies the uncertainty calculation?

Was the fitted model checked for adequacy?

What significance level will be used for the test?

For the ice cream case, the population and system were established earlier. The demand query concerns children in the target population and their flavour preferences. The time query concerns the operating system that generates waiting times between consecutive customers. The parameters are also familiar:

\[ \pi = \Pr(D_i = 1), \]

where \(D_i = 1\) means that the \(i\)th sampled child prefers chocolate, and

\[ \mu = \mathbb{E}(T_j), \]

where \(T_j\) is a waiting time in minutes between consecutive customers.

The point estimates were obtained by MLE:

\[ \hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}} = \bar{d}, \qquad \hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}} = \bar{t}. \]

The inference in this section is frequentist. Under this view, \(\pi\) and \(\mu\) are fixed but unknown quantities. The randomness comes from the sampling process, which means that the estimates, standard errors, test statistics, and confidence intervals would change if we repeated the sampling process. This viewpoint matches the sequence we have been building throughout the chapter. We first defined random variables and probability models, then described random sampling, then studied how estimators vary across repeated samples. In that repeated-sampling framework, a confidence interval is not interpreted as assigning probability to the parameter itself. Instead, the confidence level describes the long-run performance of the interval-building procedure across repeated samples.

Definition of frequentist statistics

Frequentist statistics treats unknown parameters as fixed but unknown quantities. Randomness comes from the data-generating process, the sampling process, or the experimental assignment mechanism. Under this view, probabilities describe the long-run behaviour of random quantities under repeated use of the same procedure. For example, a standard error describes how much an estimator would vary across repeated random samples, and a confidence level describes the long-run coverage behaviour of an interval procedure.

The definition above gives the frequentist interpretation we will use throughout this section. It is worth pausing, however, because many of us have also seen Bayesian language in data science settings, and the two frameworks can sound similar at first. Both use probability models and likelihoods, but they assign different roles to parameters, data, and probability. The following tip provides a brief contrast to make the frequentist workflow below easier to interpret.

Tip on frequentist and Bayesian inference!

Frequentist and Bayesian inference both use probability models, but they organize uncertainty differently. In the frequentist framework used in this cookbook, an unknown parameter such as \(\pi\) or \(\mu\) is treated as fixed but unknown. The data are random because they could have been different under repeated sampling. This is why frequentist inference focuses on sampling distributions, standard errors, confidence intervals with long-run coverage, and \(p\)-values computed under a null hypothesis.

In a Bayesian framework, uncertainty about an unknown parameter is represented directly using probability distributions. Here, let \(\theta\) denote a generic unknown parameter, such as \(\pi\) in the demand query or \(\mu\) in the time query. Let \(\mathbf{y}\) denote the observed data vector, such as the observed flavour-preference sample or the observed waiting-time sample. We begin with a prior distribution \(p(\theta)\), which represents uncertainty about \(\theta\) before observing the current data. We then combine this prior with the likelihood \(p(\mathbf{y}\mid\theta)\), which summarizes how compatible the observed data are with different possible values of \(\theta\) under the model. The result is the posterior distribution \(p(\theta\mid\mathbf{y})\), which summarizes uncertainty about \(\theta\) after observing the data. The update from prior to posterior is based on Bayes’ rule. The basic structure is

\[ \text{posterior} \propto \text{likelihood} \times \text{prior}, \]

or more explicitly,

\[ p(\theta \mid \mathbf{y}) \propto p(\mathbf{y} \mid \theta)p(\theta). \]

This chapter focuses on the frequentist route because it prepares the standard-error, confidence-interval, and hypothesis-testing tools used throughout many classical regression models. For a friendly and applied introduction to the Bayesian route, see the Bayes Rules! textbook by Johnson, Ott, and Dogucu (2022)

Under a frequentist approach, this is where the CLT work from Section 2.2.8 becomes essential. Earlier, we used the CLT to approximate the sampling distributions of estimators such as \(\hat{\pi}\) and \(\hat{\mu}\). Now, those approximations become the engine behind hypothesis tests and confidence intervals. The CLT lets us compare an observed estimate with a benchmark by measuring the gap in standard-error units:

  • For the demand query, the CLT gives an approximate Normal sampling distribution for the sample proportion.
  • For the time query, the CLT gives an approximate Normal sampling distribution for the sample mean.

This does not mean that the original data are Normal. The demand data are binary, and the waiting-time data are positive and right-skewed. The CLT is about the sampling distribution of the estimator, not the distribution of the individual observations.

Having said all this, the general settings for this subsection are summarized in Table 2.25.

A statistical hypothesis is the formal way to turn an inquiry into a statement about parameters. The definition below is written generally because later regression chapters will often involve several parameters at once. For now, the ice cream examples involve one parameter at a time.

Definition of statistical hypothesis

A statistical hypothesis is a statement about an unknown population or system parameter. Suppose a probability model contains a parameter vector

\[ \boldsymbol{\theta} = (\theta_1,\theta_2,\ldots,\theta_k)^{\top}, \]

with parameter space \(\Theta\). A statistical hypothesis restricts the possible values of \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) to a subset of that parameter space:

\[ H \text{: } \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta^*, \qquad \Theta^* \subseteq \Theta. \]

In applied work, this mathematical statement should also have a plain-language interpretation connected to the data science inquiry.

Once we have the general idea of a statistical hypothesis, we can distinguish the two hypotheses used in a classical hypothesis test. The null hypothesis provides the reference claim, while the alternative hypothesis describes the kind of departure from that reference claim that we want the test to detect. At this stage of the workflow, we only need the general definitions. The ice cream-specific hypotheses will be written in the next subsection, where we translate the stakeholder inquiries into parameter statements.

Definition of null hypothesis

The null hypothesis, denoted by \(H_0\), is the reference statement about a population or system parameter. It often represents a status quo, benchmark value, absence of effect, or simpler model. Mathematically, if the parameter vector is \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\in\Theta\), the null hypothesis can be written as

\[ H_0 \text{: } \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_0, \qquad \Theta_0 \subseteq \Theta. \]

In practice, the null hypothesis should be translated into plain language so that the statistical test remains connected to the original inquiry.

The null hypothesis is not necessarily the claim we believe is true. It is the claim we use as the reference condition for the test. This distinction is necessary because the \(p\)-value, the critical value, and the null distribution are all computed under this reference condition.

Definition of alternative hypothesis

The alternative hypothesis, denoted by \(H_1\) or \(H_a\), is the competing statement about the parameter. It describes the kind of departure from the null hypothesis that the test is designed to detect. If the null hypothesis restricts the parameter to \(\Theta_0\), the alternative hypothesis often restricts it to another part of the parameter space:

\[ H_1 \text{: } \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_1, \qquad \Theta_1 \subseteq \Theta. \]

For one-sided tests, \(\Theta_1\) specifies a direction, such as values greater than a benchmark or values less than a target. On the other hand, for two-sided tests, \(\Theta_1\) specifies departures from the null value in either direction, such as values that are either smaller or larger than the benchmark.

Note that the alternative hypothesis determines what counts as “more extreme” evidence:

  • For a right-tailed test, large positive values of the test statistic support the alternative hypothesis.
  • For a left-tailed test, large negative values support the alternative hypothesis.
  • For a two-sided test, values far from the null value in either direction support the alternative hypothesis.

Now, a hypothesis test is then the procedure that uses data to evaluate evidence against a reference hypothesis. Note that a frequentist test does not compute the probability that the null hypothesis is true. Instead, it asks whether the observed data are unusual when the null hypothesis is used as the reference condition. This is why hypothesis testing needs a clear workflow. Before we compute anything, we first decide what claim will serve as the reference point, what kind of departure would matter, and how much evidence we will require before rejecting the reference claim. Without these choices, a test statistic or \(p\)-value is just a number without an inferential role.

Definition of hypothesis testing

Hypothesis testing is a frequentist decision procedure that uses sample data to assess evidence against a null hypothesis.

A hypothesis test compares the observed data with what would be expected under the null hypothesis. The outcome is not a statement that the null hypothesis is true or false with certainty. Instead, it is a conclusion about whether the observed evidence is strong enough, according to the chosen significance level and test procedure, to reject the null hypothesis.

Because a hypothesis test makes a decision from a random sample, the decision can be wrong. This is not a flaw in the workflow; it is a consequence of using sample data to reason about an unknown population or system parameter. Frequentist testing makes these possible errors explicit:

  • A Type I error happens when the test rejects the null hypothesis even though the null hypothesis is true.
  • A Type II error happens when the test fails to reject the null hypothesis even though the alternative hypothesis is true in a scientifically or practically relevant way.

Definition of Type I error

A Type I error occurs when a test rejects the null hypothesis \(H_0\) even though \(H_0\) is true. In plain language, this is a false positive: the test concludes that there is enough evidence against the null hypothesis, but the null hypothesis is actually true.

Definition of Type II error

A Type II error occurs when a test fails to reject the null hypothesis \(H_0\) even though \(H_0\) is false in a scientifically or practically relevant way. In plain language, this is a false negative: the test does not detect a departure from the null hypothesis, even though such a departure is actually present.

Table 2.26 makes clear that Type I and Type II errors are about the relationship between the test decision and the unknown truth about \(H_0\). Since we only observe a random sample, we cannot know from a single study which cell of the table we are actually in. Instead, frequentist testing studies the long-run behaviour of the testing procedure.

Once a hypothesis test has been framed, the next question is how strict the decision rule should be. In practice, we need to decide how much evidence is enough before we are willing to reject the null hypothesis, namely \(H_0\). This is where the significance level enters, it focuses on the first column of Table 2.26. It controls how often the testing procedure would reject \(H_0\) when \(H_0\) is true. This is why the significance level is chosen before the test result is inspected. Moreover, it is important to stress that the significance level must not be chosen after seeing whether the inferential result is convenient.

Definition of significance level

The significance level, denoted by \(\alpha\), is the long-run probability of rejecting the null hypothesis (\(H_0\)) when the null hypothesis is true:

\[ \alpha = \Pr(\text{Reject } H_0 \mid H_0 \text{ is true}). \]

In plain language, \(\alpha\) is the test procedure’s tolerated Type I error rate. A smaller \(\alpha\) makes it harder to reject \(H_0\) and reduces the long-run probability of a Type I error, at the cost of making the test less sensitive to some alternatives.

For the ice cream case, choosing a significance level also has a practical interpretation. In the demand query, for example, rejecting

\[ H_0 \text{: } \pi=0.50 \]

could influence inventory decisions by encouraging the company to stock more chocolate. In the time query, for instance, rejecting

\[ H_0 \text{: } \mu=12 \]

could support the claim that the service target is being met on average. In both cases, rejecting too easily could lead to an overconfident business decision. Choosing \(\alpha\) is therefore not just a mathematical convention; it reflects how cautious we want the testing procedure to be.

Making \(\alpha\) smaller reduces the chance of rejecting a true null hypothesis, but it also makes the test more conservative. A more conservative test may miss departures from the null that are practically meaningful. This trade-off is one of the reasons hypothesis testing should be connected back to the original data science question rather than treated as a purely automatic calculation. This leads naturally to power. While the significance level describes the behaviour of the test when the null hypothesis is true, power describes the behaviour of the test when a particular alternative hypothesis is true. In other words, power asks whether the study has a good chance of detecting a signal of a given size if that signal is really present:

  • For the demand query, a power question might be: how many children would we need to survey to have a high probability of detecting that \(\pi=0.60\) rather than \(\pi=0.50\)?
  • For the time query, a power question might be: how many waiting times would we need to observe to have a high probability of detecting that \(\mu=11\) minutes rather than the service target \(\mu=12\) minutes?

The above questions are not answered by the observed \(p\)-value after the fact. They belong to the study-design side of the workflow.

Definition of power

The power (also known as the true positive rate) of a hypothesis test is the probability of rejecting the null hypothesis (\(H_0\)) when a particular alternative is true:

\[ \operatorname{Power} = \Pr(\text{Reject } H_0 \mid H_1 \text{ is true in a specified way}). \]

If \(\beta\) denotes the probability of a Type II error for a specified alternative, then

\[ \operatorname{Power} = 1-\beta. \]

Power depends on the sample size, significance level, variability, test direction, and effect size that the study aims to detect.

Note that power focuses on the second column of Table 2.26. It asks how often the test would correctly reject \(H_0\) when a specified alternative is true. Since Type II error is the failure to reject in that situation, power is the complement of the Type II error probability. Furthermore, power is not a single universal property of a test. It depends on the alternative hypothesis we care about. A test might have high power to detect a large departure from the null but low power to detect a smaller departure. This is why power calculations require an effect size: we need to specify how large a difference would matter in the scientific, operational, or business context. In the ice cream case, this distinction is important for stakeholder communication. For example, a tiny increase above one-half in chocolate preference might be statistically detectable with a very large sample, but not large enough to justify major inventory changes. Conversely, a practically important difference might fail to reach statistical significance if the sample is too small or too variable. Power analysis helps connect these statistical and practical concerns before data collection.

In the ice cream case, power belongs to the planning side of the workflow. Before collecting flavour-preference or waiting-time data, the team would ideally decide what kind of signal would be practically meaningful, how much Type I error risk is acceptable, and how likely the study should be to detect that signal if it is really present. Only after those choices are made should the team determine the required sample size.

Tip on choosing power before collecting data!

Power is usually chosen before data collection as part of study planning. A common target is

\[ \operatorname{Power}=0.80, \]

which means that the test is designed to reject \(H_0\) in about \(80\%\) of repeated studies when a specified practically meaningful alternative is true:

  • For the ice cream demand query, this would mean choosing a meaningful departure from \(\pi=0.50\), such as \(\pi=0.60\), before deciding how many children to survey.
  • For the time query, it could mean choosing a meaningful departure from the 12-minute target, such as \(\mu=11\) minutes, before deciding how many waiting times to observe.

The key point is that power is not something we choose after seeing the observed \(p\)-value. It is part of the design logic that helps determine whether the study is capable of answering the inferential inquiry.

Once a target power has been chosen, power analysis turns the planning question into a sample-size question. It asks how much data are needed for the test to have the desired probability of detecting the specified effect. In this chapter, we will not carry out a full power analysis for the ice cream case. Our goal is to understand how CLT-based hypothesis tests and confidence intervals work once the data have already been collected. Still, power analysis belongs in the general settings stage because it reminds us that inference is shaped by design choices made before the test is computed. A well-executed test cannot fully compensate for a study design that was too small or too noisy to answer the original question.

Definition of power analysis

Power analysis is a planning tool used before data collection to determine how large a sample should be for a hypothesis test to have a desired probability of detecting a practically meaningful effect. A power analysis typically requires a significance level, a target power, a test flavour, and an effect size. It belongs in the planning side of the workflow because it helps decide whether the study design is capable of answering the inferential inquiry before the data are collected.

Finally, with all these general settings in place, we can now move from abstract hypothesis-testing language to the ice cream case. The next subsection translates the stakeholder inquiries into concrete null and alternative hypotheses for the demand and time queries.

2.5.2 Hypotheses Definitions

The hypotheses definitions stage, shown in Figure 2.16, is where we translate the stakeholder inquiries into statistical statements about parameters. The general language of \(H_0\) and \(H_1\) is now in place. Therefore, we can focus on the ice cream case itself:

What claim should be treated as the reference condition, and what kind of departure would answer the stakeholder’s question?

This translation step is more important than it might look. A stakeholder will usually ask a question in plain language, such as

Do children prefer chocolate?

or

Are wait times below our target?

A statistical hypothesis test requires a sharper version of that question. We need a parameter, a benchmark value, and a direction of evidence. Without this sharpening, the test statistic and \(p\)-value will not have a clear interpretation.

As indicated in Table 2.24, or the demand query, the stakeholder is not asking whether the chocolate-preference probability is merely different from one half. The business question has a direction: the company wants evidence that chocolate is preferred by more than half of the target population. This makes the alternative hypothesis right-sided:

\[ H_0 \text{: } \pi = 0.50 \qquad \text{versus} \qquad H_1 \text{: } \pi > 0.50. \]

For the time query, the stakeholder is not asking whether the mean waiting time merely differs from the service target. The operational question also has a direction: the team wants evidence that the average waiting time is below the 12-minute target. This makes the alternative hypothesis left-sided:

\[ H_0 \text{: } \mu = 12 \qquad \text{versus} \qquad H_1 \text{: } \mu < 12. \]

In both cases, the null hypothesis is written with equality because it provides the precise reference value used to construct the null distribution. The alternative hypothesis then determines which tail of the null distribution will be used to compute the \(p\)-value. This is why the direction in \(H_1\) is not just cosmetic; it changes the inferential calculation.

Heads-up on why \(H_0\) is not written as the full complement of \(H_1\)!

At first glance, it might seem that the null hypothesis, \(H_0\), should be the exact complement of the alternative hypothesis, \(H_1\). For example, if

\[ H_1 \text{: } \pi > 0.50, \]

then one might expect the null hypothesis to be

\[ H_0 \text{: } \pi \leq 0.50. \]

However, in many classical hypothesis tests, we write the null hypothesis using the boundary value:

\[ H_0 \text{: } \pi = 0.50. \]

This boundary value provides the sharp reference point used to construct the null distribution. For the demand query, the test asks whether the observed sample proportion is unusually large compared with what we would expect if \(\pi=0.50\). If the sample is unusual relative to this boundary value, it will also be unusual relative to smaller values of \(\pi\) for a right-tailed test.

Similarly, for the time query, we write

\[ H_0 \text{: } \mu = 12 \]

rather than

\[ H_0 \text{: } \mu \geq 12. \]

The value \(\mu=12\) is the boundary between meeting and not meeting the service target. It gives us the reference distribution for judging whether the observed average waiting time is unusually small. Thus, the equality null is a practical and mathematical way to test evidence at the boundary between the null-side and alternative-side claims.

Table 2.27 also makes clear why both tests are one-sided. For the demand query, evidence in favour of the alternative means observing an estimated chocolate-preference probability sufficiently above \(0.50\). For the time query, evidence in favour of the alternative means observing an estimated average waiting time sufficiently below 12 minutes. These directions determine which tail of the null distribution will be used when we compute the corresponding \(p\)-values.

Now, the confidence-interval inquiries (from Table 2.28) are organized separately because they are not directional tests. A confidence interval is used to quantify uncertainty around a point estimate. For the demand query, we will build a two-sided interval for \(\pi\) around \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\). For the time query, we will build a two-sided interval for \(\mu\) around \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\). This separation avoids a common source of confusion:

  • The one-sided tests answer decision-oriented questions about whether the data provide evidence in a specific direction.
  • The two-sided confidence intervals answer estimation-oriented questions about how much uncertainty surrounds the point estimates. Both are useful, but they are not the same inferential object.

The next step is to choose the test flavour and identify the components needed to carry out these tests. Because this is Chapter 2, we will use the CLT-based Normal approximations developed in Section 2.2.8. Later regression chapters will use the same workflow with model-specific test statistics and standard errors.

2.5.3 Test Flavour and Components

The test flavour and components stage, from Figure 2.17, specifies how the hypotheses will be tested. For the ice cream case, both tests use CLT-based Normal approximations. This choice follows directly from Section 2.2.8, where we studied how sample proportions and sample means can have approximately Normal sampling distributions even when the original observations are not Normal.

That said, note that the demand observations are binary, and the waiting-time observations are positive and right-skewed. We are not pretending that individual observations are Normal. Instead, we are using the CLT to approximate the sampling distribution of the estimator. In other words, the Normal curve below will describe the behaviour of a standardized estimator across repeated samples, not the shape of the raw data.

The generic CLT-based test statistic has the form

\[ \text{test statistic} = \frac{ \text{observed estimate} - \text{null value} }{ \text{standard error} }. \]

The numerator is the observed effect: how far the observed estimate is from the null value. Then, the denominator is the standard error: the scale of sampling variability. Dividing by the standard error expresses the observed effect in standard-error units. This is what allows us to place the result on a reference distribution such as the standard Normal distribution.

Definition of observed effect

An observed effect is the difference between an observed estimate and the value of the parameter specified by the null hypothesis. For a scalar parameter \(\theta\), an observed effect often has the form

\[ \hat{\theta}_{\operatorname{obs}} - \theta_0, \]

where \(\hat{\theta}_{\operatorname{obs}}\) is the estimate computed from the observed sample and \(\theta_0\) is the null value.

The observed effect alone is not enough to judge evidence. For example, a difference of \(0.03\) may be large in a very precise study and small in a noisy study. This is why the standard error is central. It tells us the repeated-sampling scale on which the observed effect should be evaluated.

Definition of standard error in inference

A standard error measures the repeated-sampling variability of an estimator. In hypothesis testing, the standard error is used to judge whether the observed effect is large or small relative to the variability expected under the sampling process.

For CLT-based tests, the standard error is the scaling quantity that converts an observed effect into a standardized test statistic.

Once the test statistic has been standardized, we compare it with its null distribution. The null distribution acts like a reference map. It tells us where test statistics would tend to fall if the null hypothesis were the correct reference condition. Values near the centre are ordinary under \(H_0\). Values far into the tail specified by \(H_1\) are unusual under \(H_0\).

Definition of null distribution

The null distribution is the probability distribution of a test statistic computed under the assumption that the null hypothesis is true. It tells us what values of the test statistic would be ordinary or unusual if the null hypothesis were the correct reference condition.

Definition of test statistic

A test statistic is a numerical summary of the observed sample used to measure how far the observed evidence is from the reference behaviour implied by the null hypothesis.

A common CLT-based test statistic has the form

\[ \text{Test statistic} = \frac{ \text{observed estimate} - \text{null value} }{ \text{standard error} }. \]

When the CLT approximation is appropriate, this statistic is often compared to a standard Normal distribution under the null hypothesis.

For the demand query, the null value is \(\pi_0=0.50\). Since we are testing a claim about the population chocolate-preference probability, the CLT suggests standardizing the difference between the observed sample proportion \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) and the reference value \(\pi_0\) by its standard error. Under the null hypothesis, the reference value is

\[ \pi_0 = 0.50. \]

Because the demand response is modelled as Bernoulli,

\[ D_i \sim \operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi), \]

we have

\[ \mathbb{E}(D_i)=\pi \qquad \text{and} \qquad \operatorname{Var}(D_i)=\pi(1-\pi). \]

Since

\[ \hat{\pi} = \frac{1}{n_d} \sum_{i=1}^{n_d}D_i, \]

the null-implied standard error of the sample proportion is

\[ \sqrt{ \frac{ \pi_0(1-\pi_0) }{n_d} }. \]

This gives the CLT-based test statistic

\[ Z_{\pi} = \frac{ \hat{\pi}-\pi_0 }{ \sqrt{\pi_0(1-\pi_0)/n_d} } \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1) \quad \text{under } H_0. \tag{2.63}\]

Heads-up on why we use the null standard error for the demand test!

For the demand query, the standard error in the hypothesis test is already model-based under the null hypothesis. Since the Bernoulli variance is

\[ \operatorname{Var}(D_i)=\pi(1-\pi), \]

the null hypothesis

\[ H_0 \text{: }\pi=\pi_0 \]

directly implies the null standard error

\[ \sqrt{ \frac{ \pi_0(1-\pi_0) }{n_d} }. \]

This is why the one-proportion CLT-based test uses \(\pi_0\) in the denominator, not the observed estimate \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\). The test is asking what kinds of sample proportions would be expected if the null value were true.

Later, when we build a confidence interval for \(\pi\), the goal changes. We are no longer testing a specific null value. Instead, we are quantifying uncertainty around the observed estimate, so the confidence interval uses the plug-in standard error based on \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\).

For the time query, the null value is \(\mu_0=12\) minutes. Since we are testing a claim about the population mean waiting time, the CLT suggests standardizing the difference between the observed sample mean \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) and the reference value \(\mu_0\) by its standard error.

Under the null hypothesis, the reference value is

\[ \mu_0 = 12. \]

In a general mean-based test, the standard error of the sample mean is estimated using the sample standard deviation \(s_T\):

\[ \frac{s_T}{\sqrt{n_t}}. \]

This gives the CLT-based test statistic

\[ Z_{\mu} = \frac{ \hat{\mu}-\mu_0 }{ s_T/\sqrt{n_t} } \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1) \quad \text{under } H_0. \tag{2.64}\]

Heads-up on the Exponential model and the standard error!

The time query has a special characteristic because we previously modelled the waiting times using an Exponential distribution with scale parameter \(\mu\). Under that model,

\[ \mathbb{E}(T_j)=\mu \qquad \text{and} \qquad \operatorname{SD}(T_j)=\mu. \]

Therefore, if the Exponential model is taken literally under the null hypothesis \(\mu_0=12\), the model-implied standard error of the sample mean would be

\[ \frac{\mu_0}{\sqrt{n_t}} = \frac{12}{\sqrt{n_t}}. \]

In this section, we instead use the more general CLT-based plug-in standard error

\[ \frac{s_T}{\sqrt{n_t}}, \]

where \(s_T\) is the sample standard deviation of the observed waiting times. This choice keeps the test aligned with the general mean-inference workflow: estimate the sampling variability of the sample mean from the observed data.

For the simulated ice cream data, both approaches are connected. If the Exponential model fits well and the sample size is reasonably large, \(s_T\) should be close to the fitted mean scale \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\), and the two standard errors should be similar. Large disagreement between \(s_T\) and \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) would itself be a useful warning sign for goodness of fit.

In both Equation 2.63 and Equation 2.64, the notation \(\dot{\sim}\) is the same practical approximation notation used in Section 2.2.8. It reminds us that these are CLT-based approximations rather than exact finite-sample distributions. Finally, Table 2.29 shows why the two tests have the same general structure but different directions. The demand query looks for unusually large values of \(Z_{\pi}\), while the time query looks for unusually small values of \(Z_{\mu}\). This direction will determine the shaded tail in the null-distribution plots.

2.5.4 Inferential Conclusions

Let us proceed with the next stage in our inferential workflow, as shown in Figure 2.18. Once we have a test statistic and its null distribution, we can compute a \(p\)-value. This concept is often confusing because the \(p\)-value is not a probability about whether the null hypothesis is true. It is a probability about the test statistic, computed under the null hypothesis.

Definition of \(p\)-value

A \(p\)-value is the probability, computed under the null hypothesis, of observing a test statistic as extreme as or more extreme than the one obtained from the observed sample, in the direction specified by the alternative hypothesis. The \(p\)-value is not the probability that the null hypothesis is true. It is a probability about the test statistic under the null hypothesis.

A useful way to read a \(p\)-value is to imagine a repeated-sampling world where the null distribution, \(H_0\), is used as the reference condition. In that world, each repeated sample would produce a new test statistic. The null distribution describes where those test statistics would tend to fall. The \(p\)-value asks how often that null-world process would produce a statistic as extreme as the one we observed. Note that the phrase “as extreme as” depends on the alternative hypothesis. For the demand query, the alternative is \(H_1\text{: }\pi>0.50\), so large positive values of the test statistic support the alternative. Therefore, the \(p\)-value is a right-tail probability:

\[ p\text{-value} = \Pr(Z_{\pi} \geq z_{\pi,\operatorname{obs}}) \quad \text{under } H_0. \]

For the time query, the alternative is \(H_1\text{: }\mu<12\), so large negative values of the test statistic support the alternative. Therefore, the \(p\)-value is a left-tail probability:

\[ p\text{-value} = \Pr(Z_{\mu} \leq z_{\mu,\operatorname{obs}}) \quad \text{under } H_0. \]

Heads-up on what a \(p\)-value is not!

A \(p\)-value is not the probability that \(H_0\) is true. It is also not the probability that the result was caused by chance. In this frequentist workflow, \(H_0\) is used as the reference condition. Hence, the \(p\)-value asks how unusual the observed test statistic would be if that reference condition were true.

The \(p\)-value decision rule is consistent across test directions:

\[ \text{Reject } H_0 \quad \text{if} \quad p\text{-value}\leq \alpha. \]

If the \(p\)-value is larger than the significance level \(\alpha\), we fail to reject \(H_0\):

\[ \text{Fail to reject } H_0 \quad \text{if} \quad p\text{-value}>\alpha. \]

The phrase “fail to reject” is intentionally cautious. It means that the observed sample did not provide enough evidence to reject the null hypothesis under the chosen test. It does not mean that the null hypothesis has been proven true!

Now, an equivalent decision can be made using critical values. In the critical-value approach, the significance level \(\alpha\) is used to define a rejection region in the tail of the null distribution.

Definition of critical value

A critical value is a cutoff from the null distribution used to decide whether the observed test statistic falls in the rejection region. For a test with significance level \(\alpha\), the critical value is chosen so that the rejection region has probability \(\alpha\) under the null hypothesis.

The same inferential decision logic can be expressed in two equivalent ways:

  1. The \(p\)-value rule compares the observed tail probability with the significance level \(\alpha\).
  2. The critical-value rule compares the observed test statistic with a cutoff from the null distribution.

These two approaches lead to the same reject-or-fail-to-reject decision when they are based on the same null distribution, alternative hypothesis, and significance level. Table 2.30 summarizes the decision rules for common Normal-approximation tests. The important detail is that the rejection region depends on the direction of the alternative hypothesis. A right-tailed test looks for unusually large positive values of \(z_{\operatorname{obs}}\), a left-tailed test looks for unusually small negative values, and a two-sided test looks for values far from zero in either direction.

For the ice cream case, we will use the first two rows of Table 2.30. The demand query is right-tailed because the alternative is \(H_1 \text{: }\pi>0.50\). On the other hand, the time query is left-tailed because the alternative is \(H_1 \text{: }\mu<12\). The two-sided row will become useful when we discuss confidence intervals and, later in the cookbook, two-sided tests for regression coefficients.

The next two subsections compute the ice cream hypothesis tests. The numerical computations are shown in both R and Python because the simulated observed samples differ across languages in this chapter (see Section 2.1.5).

Demand Query: Testing Whether Chocolate Preference Exceeds One Half

For the demand query, the one-sided inferential inquiry is:

Does the sample provide evidence that the population chocolate-preference probability is larger than one half?

The hypotheses are

\[ H_0\text{: }\pi = 0.50 \]

versus

\[ H_1\text{: }\pi > 0.50. \]

Under \(H_0\), the observed CLT-based test statistic is

\[ z_{\pi,\operatorname{obs}} = \frac{ \hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}-0.50 }{ \sqrt{0.50(1-0.50)/n_d} }. \]

The numerator is the observed difference between the estimated chocolate-preference probability and one half. The denominator is the null standard error of the sample proportion. We use the null standard error here because the test asks what kinds of sample proportions would be expected if \(\pi_0=0.50\) were true.

The following code, via Listing 2.1 and Listing 2.2, computes the test statistic and one-sided \(p\)-value using the observed demand sample. The calculation uses the unrounded sample proportion, even if rounded values are displayed elsewhere for presentation.

Heads-up on why the R and Python inferential results may differ!

The inferential results in the subsequent R and Python tabs may not be numerically identical. This is expected since, earlier in the chapter, we simulated the populations and observed random samples separately in R and Python. Even when the same seed is used, the two languages rely on different random number generators. As a result, the observed samples can differ across languages, and so can the corresponding point estimates, standard errors, test statistics, \(p\)-values, confidence intervals, and null-distribution figures.

That said, the inferential workflow is the same in both languages. What changes is the particular observed random sample being analyzed. For this reason, the numerical tables should be interpreted within each language tab, while the displayed null-distribution figures (Figure 2.19 and Figure 2.20) correspond to the R-based observed samples.

  • R
  • Python
  n_d chocolate_count pi_hat_obs se_null  z_obs p_value critical_value
1 500             336      0.672  0.0224 7.6921       0         1.6449
  reject_h0
1      TRUE
n_d chocolate_count pi_hat_obs se_null z_obs p_value critical_value reject_h0
0 500 307 0.614 0.0224 5.0982 0.0 1.6449 True

The above code output provides the numerical details of the test. The plot in Figure 2.19 shows the same information, for the R-based observed sample, but graphically. The centre of the curve at \(0\), on the horizontal axis, corresponds to values of the test statistic that are ordinary under \(H_0\). The right tail corresponds to values that support the alternative \(H_1:\pi>0.50\). The dashed vertical line marks the observed test statistic, the dotted vertical line marks the critical value, and the shaded area (practically non-visible) represents the \(p\)-value.

As indicated in Table 2.30, if the \(p\)-value is less than or equal to \(\alpha=0.05\), we reject \(H_0\) and conclude that the sample provides evidence that the chocolate-preference probability is larger than one half. On the other hand, if the \(p\)-value is greater than \(\alpha=0.05\), we fail to reject \(H_0\). In that case, we say that the sample does not provide enough evidence, under this test, to conclude that chocolate is preferred by more than half of children in the target population.

For this demand query, the technical interpretation should be tied directly to the null distribution in Figure 2.19. The observed test statistic \(z_{\pi,\operatorname{obs}}\) measures how many null standard errors the observed sample proportion \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) is above the benchmark value \(\pi_0=0.50\). The right-tail \(p\)-value then measures how often, under the reference condition \(H_0 \text{: }\pi=0.50\), we would observe a standardized sample proportion at least as large as the one obtained from the sample. Thus, a small right-tail \(p\)-value (and less than \(\alpha = 0.05\), such as in this query) means that the observed chocolate-preference proportion is unusually large relative to what the null hypothesis would lead us to expect. Since we reject \(H_0\) at \(\alpha=0.05\), the evidence supports the directional claim that \(\pi>0.50\) under the Bernoulli sampling model and CLT approximation.

Time Query: Testing Whether Average Waiting Time Is Below the Service Target

For the time query, the one-sided inferential inquiry is:

Does the sample provide evidence that the population average waiting time is below the service target of 12 minutes?

The hypotheses are

\[ H_0\text{: }\mu = 12 \]

versus

\[ H_1\text{: }\mu < 12. \]

Under \(H_0\), the observed CLT-based test statistic is

\[ z_{\mu,\operatorname{obs}} = \frac{ \hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}-12 }{ s_T/\sqrt{n_t} }. \]

where \(s_T\) is the sample standard deviation of the observed waiting times. In this test, smaller values of the sample mean support the alternative hypothesis. Therefore, the relevant tail area is on the left side of the null distribution.

The following code, via Listing 2.3 and Listing 2.4, computes the test statistic and one-sided \(p\)-value using the observed waiting-time sample.

  • R
  • Python
  n_t mu_hat_obs sample_sd se_est   z_obs p_value critical_value reject_h0
1 200    10.2314    9.3203  0.659 -2.6836  0.0036        -1.6449      TRUE
n_t mu_hat_obs sample_sd se_est z_obs p_value critical_value reject_h0
0 200 9.6685 9.533 0.6741 -3.4588 0.0003 -1.6449 True

The above code output reports the observed statistic and the left-tail \(p\)-value. The plot in Figure 2.20 shows the corresponding null distribution for the R-based observed sample. The centre of the curve at \(0\), on the horizontal axis, corresponds to values of the test statistic that are ordinary under \(H_0\). Here, the shaded tail is on the left because values far below the target support the alternative \(H_1\text{: }\mu<12\). The dashed vertical line marks the observed test statistic, the dotted vertical line marks the critical value, and the shaded area represents the \(p\)-value.

As indicated in Table 2.30, if the \(p\)-value is less than or equal to \(\alpha=0.05\), we reject \(H_0\) and conclude that the sample provides evidence that the population average waiting time is below the 12-minute service target. On the other hand, if the \(p\)-value is greater than \(\alpha=0.05\), we fail to reject \(H_0\). In that case, we say that the sample does not provide enough evidence, under this test, to conclude that the average waiting time is below 12 minutes.

For this time query, the technical interpretation should be tied directly to the null distribution in Figure 2.20. The observed test statistic \(z_{\mu,\operatorname{obs}}\) measures how many estimated standard errors the observed sample mean \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) is below the benchmark value \(\mu_0=12\) minutes. The left-tail \(p\)-value then measures how often, under the reference condition \(H_0 \text{: }\mu=12\), we would observe a standardized sample mean at least as small as the one obtained from the sample.

Thus, a small left-tail \(p\)-value (and less than \(\alpha = 0.05\), such as in this query) means that the observed average waiting time is unusually small relative to what the null hypothesis would lead us to expect. Since we reject \(H_0\) at \(\alpha=0.05\), the evidence supports the directional claim that \(\mu<12\) minutes under the CLT approximation and the mean-inference workflow used here.

Confidence Intervals as Uncertainty Quantification

The previous hypothesis tests answered two directional benchmark questions. The demand test asked whether \(\pi\) is larger than \(0.50\), and the time test asked whether \(\mu\) is below 12 minutes. Confidence intervals answer a different kind of question:

How much uncertainty surrounds the point estimates?

This distinction is important for communication. A hypothesis test can say whether a directional claim is supported at a chosen significance level, but it does not show the full range of plausible parameter values. A confidence interval gives stakeholders a more nuanced view. It helps them see whether the estimate is far from the benchmark or close to it, and whether the uncertainty is small or large enough to affect decisions.

Definition of confidence interval

A confidence interval is an interval computed from sample data using a procedure designed to capture the unknown parameter a specified proportion of the time across repeated random samples. For example, a 95% confidence interval procedure is one that, under its assumptions, would produce intervals containing the true parameter in about 95% of repeated samples. For a single observed interval, we do not say that there is a 95% probability that the fixed frequentist parameter lies inside the interval. Instead, we say that the interval was produced by a procedure with 95% long-run coverage.

For the demand query, the CLT-based 95% confidence interval for \(\pi\) is

\[ \hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}} \pm z_{0.975} \sqrt{ \frac{ \hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}} \left( 1-\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}} \right) }{n_d} }, \]

\(z_q\) denotes the \(q\)th quantile of the standard Normal distribution. This interval uses the estimated standard error because the goal is uncertainty quantification around the estimate, not testing a specific null value. That is why the standard error here uses \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) rather than \(\pi_0=0.50\).

For the time query, the CLT-based 95% confidence interval for \(\mu\) is

\[ \hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}} \pm z_{0.975} \frac{s_T}{\sqrt{n_t}}, \]

where \(z_q\) denotes the \(q\)th quantile of the standard Normal distribution.

Note that both intervals, via Listing 2.5 and Listing 2.6, are two-sided because they measure uncertainty above and below the observed estimates. The following code computes both intervals from the observed samples.

  • R
  • Python
         query parameter confidence_level estimate standard_error lower upper
1 Demand query        pi             0.95     0.67           0.02  0.63  0.71
2   Time query        mu             0.95    10.23           0.66  8.94 11.52
query parameter confidence_level estimate standard_error lower upper
0 Demand query pi 0.95 0.61 0.02 0.57 0.66
1 Time query mu 0.95 9.67 0.67 8.35 10.99

It is essential to stress that a confidence interval should be read as an uncertainty summary, not as a binary decision by itself:

  • For the demand query, the interval shows which chocolate-preference probabilities are compatible with the observed sample and CLT approximation. If the interval is narrow and mostly above one half, that supports a stronger inventory-planning story than an interval that barely crosses one half or is very wide.
  • For the time query, the interval shows whether the estimated average waiting time is clearly below the 12-minute target or close enough to the target that operational caution is still warranted.

Heads-up on confidence intervals and hypothesis tests!

Confidence intervals and hypothesis tests are connected, but they answer different questions.

The one-sided tests above ask whether the data provide enough evidence to support directional claims at a chosen significance level. The two-sided confidence intervals summarize uncertainty around the point estimates. When communicating results, it is often helpful to report both: the test addresses the decision-oriented inquiry, and the interval shows the range of values still compatible with the observed data and modelling assumptions.

Summarizing Conclusions

Let us recall that the inferential conclusions stage is where we translate \(p\)-values, critical values, and intervals into careful statistical language. This stage should avoid overclaiming. Rejecting \(H_0\) does not prove that \(H_1\) is true. Moreover, failing to reject \(H_0\) does not prove that \(H_0\) is true. Both conclusions are statements about evidence under the chosen model, sampling assumptions, test direction, and significance level.

The following table, via Listing 2.7 and Listing 2.8, brings the hypothesis-test results and confidence intervals together. At this point in the workflow, the goal is to support interpretation and communication.

  • R
  • Python
         query parameter                    hypothesis_test estimate
1 Demand query        pi H0: pi = 0.50 versus H1: pi > 0.50   0.6720
2   Time query        mu     H0: mu = 12 versus H1: mu < 12  10.2314
  test_statistic p_value reject_h0 ci_lower ci_upper
1         7.6921  0.0000      TRUE     0.63     0.71
2        -2.6836  0.0036      TRUE     8.94    11.52
query parameter hypothesis_test estimate test_statistic p_value reject_h0 ci_lower ci_upper
0 Demand query pi H0: pi = 0.50 versus H1: pi > 0.50 0.6140 5.0982 0.0000 True 0.57 0.66
1 Time query mu H0: mu = 12 versus H1: mu < 12 9.6685 -3.4588 0.0003 True 8.35 10.99

It is important to stress that a results table is helpful, but it is not the final product. The final product is a statistical story that a stakeholder can understand. That story should state the estimate, uncertainty, hypothesis-test conclusion, and assumptions in plain language.

2.5.5 Parameter Testing, Goodness-of-Fit Testing, and Model Comparison

The hypothesis tests in this inferential section are parameter tests. They ask whether a parameter is equal to a benchmark value or on one side of a benchmark. This is different from the goodness-of-fit work in Section 2.4, where the question was whether the fitted probability model looked adequate.

Note that this distinction is critical because a statistically significant parameter test does not automatically validate the model:

  • For the demand query, a parameter test asks whether \(\pi>0.50\). On the other hand, a goodness-of-fit check asks whether a single Bernoulli probability is an adequate description of the preference data.
  • For the time query, a parameter test asks whether \(\mu<12\). On the other hand, a goodness-of-fit check asks whether a single Exponential distribution is adequate for the waiting-time pattern.

This distinction indicated in Table 2.31 will return throughout the regression chapters. Testing whether a coefficient is different from zero is not the same as checking whether the model is well specified. A statistically significant coefficient can appear in a poorly fitting model, and a well-fitting model can still include coefficients that are not statistically significant.

2.5.6 Storytelling the Inferential Results

Let us now turn the inferential results back into the stakeholder-facing inquiries from Table 2.24. At this stage, the goal is no longer to re-derive the test statistics or confidence intervals. The goal is to communicate what the evidence says about the two practical questions:

Does the demand sample provide evidence that chocolate is preferred by more than half of children in the target population?

Does the waiting-time sample provide evidence that the average waiting time is below the 12-minute service target?

Because we have been reporting both R and Python computations, the storytelling below presents the R-based figures first, with the Python-based figures in brackets. The two sets of numbers may differ slightly because, as noted earlier, the two languages simulated different observed random samples even when using the same conceptual data-generating setup.

Demand Query

The demand team wanted to know whether chocolate is preferred by more than half of children in the target population. Using the observed demand sample, we estimated the chocolate-preference probability as approximately 0.67 or 67% (in Python, it is 0.61 or 61%). To address the directional inquiry, we tested \[H_0 \text{: } \pi = 0.50 \qquad \text{versus} \qquad H_1 \text{: } \pi > 0.50.\]

The one-sided Central Limit Theorem-based test gives a \(p\text{-value}<0.001\) in both R and Python. Since this value is less than or equal to the significance level \(\alpha=0.05\), we reject \(H_0\) in the inferential analysis.

Interpreted for the demand team, the observed sample provides evidence that chocolate is preferred by more than half of children in the target population. This conclusion is based on the Bernoulli sampling model and the Central Limit Theorem approximation used for the one-proportion test.

For inventory planning, the point estimate alone is not enough. The test above addresses the majority-preference question, while the confidence interval shows how much uncertainty remains around the estimated preference probability. In the inferential analysis, the 95% confidence interval for \(\pi\) is approximately (0.63, 0.71) (in Python, it is approximately between 0.57 and 0.66). Since the interval is above the practical benchmark of 0.50, this helps the demand team conclude that the observed preference for chocolate is clearly above a simple majority.

Time Query

The operations team wanted to know whether the average waiting time is below the 12-minute service target. Using the observed waiting-time sample, we estimated the average waiting time as approximately 10.23 minutes (in Python, it is 9.67 minutes). To address the directional inquiry, we tested

\[H_0 \text{: } \mu = 12 \qquad \text{versus} \qquad H_1 \text{: } \mu < 12.\] The one-sided Central Limit Theorem-based test gives a \(p\text{-value}\) of 0.0036 (in Python, it is < 0.001). Since this value is less than or equal to the significance level \(\alpha=0.05\), we reject \(H_0\) in the R-based inferential analysis (in Python, we reject \(H_0\)).

Interpreted for the operations team, the observed sample provides evidence that the average waiting time is below the 12-minute service target. This conclusion is based on the Central Limit Theorem approximation used for the mean-based test, with the sample standard deviation used to estimate uncertainty in the observed average waiting time.

For service planning, the point estimate alone is not enough. The test above addresses the directional service-target question, while the confidence interval shows how much uncertainty remains around the estimated average waiting time. In the inferential analysis, the 95% confidence interval for \(\mu\) is approximately (8.94, 11.52) minutes (in Python, it is approximately between 8.35 and 10.99 minutes). Since the interval is below the practical benchmark of 12 minutes, this helps the operations team conclude that the observed average waiting time is comfortably below the service target.

Heads-up on reporting frequentist inference!

A careful inference report should include four pieces:

  1. the point estimate,
  2. an uncertainty summary,
  3. the hypothesis-test conclusion when a benchmark is relevant, and
  4. the assumptions or modelling checks that support the conclusion.

For the ice cream case, that means reporting both the MLEs and their confidence intervals, while also stating that the hypothesis tests use CLT-based Normal approximations and rely on the fitted probability models being adequate enough for the questions at hand.

2.6 Chapter Summary

In this chapter, we used an ice cream case study to build the statistical foundation needed for the regression models that follow in the cookbook. We began by translating stakeholder questions into statistical inquiries about populations, samples, random variables, parameters, and observed estimates. The demand query focused on a binary flavour-preference variable, modelled with a Bernoulli distribution and summarized through the chocolate-preference probability \(\pi\). On the other hand, the time query focused on a continuous waiting-time variable, modelled with an Exponential distribution and summarized through the mean waiting time \(\mu\). These examples helped connect probability distributions, expected values, variance, random sampling, and sampling distributions to concrete data science questions.

Then, we showed how estimation and model assessment fit into this picture. Maximum likelihood estimation gave us a principled way to estimate unknown parameters from observed samples, producing \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) for the demand query and \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}\) for the time query. Goodness-of-fit checks then asked whether the fitted models were adequate enough for the questions being asked. For demand, the observed-versus-expected count comparison was useful as a transparent summary but limited as a stress test because the Bernoulli probability was estimated from the same sample. For time, the histogram, fitted Exponential density, ECDF comparison, and mean-versus-standard-deviation check gave us more ways to assess whether the Exponential model was reasonable.

Finally, we used the CLT to move from estimation to frequentist inference. The CLT provided approximate sampling distributions for the sample proportion and sample mean, which allowed us to construct one-sided hypothesis tests and two-sided confidence intervals. The demand test addressed whether chocolate is preferred by more than half of children in the target population, while the time test addressed whether the average waiting time is below the 12-minute service target. Storytelling translated these inferential results into stakeholder-facing conclusions with uncertainty. Therefore, this completes the inferential loop introduced in Chapter 1: define the inquiry, identify the parameter, fit the model, check goodness of fit, use an appropriate sampling distribution or approximation, and communicate the result in a way that supports decisions.

Major food for thought for the next chapters!

In this chapter, the parameters were simple: one probability \(\pi\) for the demand query and one mean \(\mu\) for the time query. In the regression chapters that follow, the same statistical workflow will remain in place, but the parameters will become more structured. Instead of estimating a single population quantity, we will often estimate a vector of \(k\) regression coefficients such as

\[ \boldsymbol{\beta} = (\beta_0,\beta_1,\ldots,\beta_k)^\top. \]

This shift changes the kinds of questions we can answer. Rather than asking only whether chocolate is preferred by more than half of children, a regression model might ask how flavour preference changes by city, age group, weather, or day of the week. Rather than asking only whether the average waiting time is below 12 minutes, a regression model might ask how waiting time changes with queue length, staffing level, cart location, or time of day.

The key idea is that regression does not replace the logic of this chapter. It extends it! We will still define an inquiry, choose a probability model, estimate unknown parameters, assess goodness of fit, use sampling distributions or approximations for inference, and communicate results with uncertainty. What changes is that the parameter now describes a relationship between variables, not just a single population feature.

2.7 Practice Exercises

The following exercises give you a chance to practice the main probability, estimation, model-checking, and inference ideas developed in this chapter using applied settings beyond the ice cream case study. The questions are organized by topic, but the question types are intentionally mixed: multiple-choice questions build fast recognition, true/false questions test precision in statistical language, and open-ended questions ask you to build complete mathematical and interpretive arguments. Across the exercises, you will work with several probability distributions that reappear throughout the cookbook, including Bernoulli, Binomial, Poisson, Negative Binomial, Gamma, Weibull, Lognormal, Logistic, Normal, Beta, Zero-inflated Poisson, and Multinomial models (for further information, check Appendix C). The goal is not only to compute answers, but also to connect notation, assumptions, likelihoods, sampling distributions, goodness-of-fit checks, hypothesis tests, confidence intervals, and stakeholder-facing interpretation.

2.7.1 Why Probability Belongs in a Regression Cookbook

Question 2.1

Multiple Choice

A public-health dashboard records whether a clinic visit results in a follow-up appointment. Let

\[ F_i = \begin{cases} 1, & \text{if visit } i \text{ results in a follow-up appointment,}\\ 0, & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases} \]

Suppose \(F_i \sim \operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi)\), where \(\pi=\Pr(F_i=1)\). Which expression is the correct PMF using the notation conventions from this chapter?

A. \(p_F(f_i \mid \pi)=\pi^{f_i}(1-\pi)^{1-f_i}\), for \(f_i\in\{0,1\}\).

B. \(p_F(f_i;\pi)=\pi^{f_i}(1-\pi)^{1-f_i}\), for \(f_i\in\{0,1\}\).

C. \(f_F(f_i;\pi)=\pi^{f_i}(1-\pi)^{1-f_i}\), for \(f_i\in[0,1]\).

D. \(p_F(f_i;\pi)=f_i^{\pi}(1-f_i)^{1-\pi}\), for \(\pi\in\{0,1\}\).

Answer 2.1

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

The Bernoulli PMF is

\[ p_F(f_i;\pi)=\pi^{f_i}(1-\pi)^{1-f_i}, \qquad f_i\in\{0,1\}. \]

Option B also follows the chapter convention of using a semicolon to separate the random-variable value from the distributional parameter. Option A uses conditional notation where parameter-indexed PMF notation is intended, Option C uses density notation and the wrong support, and Option D reverses the roles of the data value and the parameter.

Question 2.2

True or False

A count variable \(Y\) that records the number of software errors found in a fixed one-hour testing window can be modelled as \(Y\sim\operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda)\) only if its support is finite.

Answer 2.2

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

A Poisson random variable has count support

\[ y\in\{0,1,2,\ldots\}, \]

which is countably infinite, not finite (you can check Section D.4). The Poisson model is often used for event counts in a fixed interval of time or space, provided the modelling assumptions are reasonable for the application.

Question 2.3

Open-ended Question

A software reliability team models the number of bugs \(Y\) found in a fixed testing window as

\[ Y\sim\operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda), \]

with PMF

\[ p_Y(y;\lambda)=\frac{\exp(-\lambda)\lambda^y}{y!}, \qquad y\in\{0,1,2,\ldots\}, \quad \lambda>0. \]

Show that this PMF sums to one over its support. Then explain why this matters before using the model in an applied analysis.

Answer 2.3

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

We need to show that

\[ \sum_{y=0}^{\infty}p_Y(y;\lambda)=1. \]

Using the Poisson PMF,

\[ \begin{aligned} \sum_{y=0}^{\infty}p_Y(y;\lambda) &= \sum_{y=0}^{\infty}\frac{\exp(-\lambda)\lambda^y}{y!}\\ &= \exp(-\lambda)\sum_{y=0}^{\infty}\frac{\lambda^y}{y!}\\ &= \exp(-\lambda)\exp(\lambda)\\ &=1. \end{aligned} \]

This matters because a PMF must assign valid probabilities over all possible outcomes. If the probabilities did not sum to one, the model would not define a valid probability distribution for the count process.

Question 2.4

Multiple Choice

A manufacturing team models the proportion of a surface covered by a coating defect as

\[ Y\sim\operatorname{Beta}(\alpha,\beta), \]

where \(0<Y<1\), \(\alpha>0\), and \(\beta>0\). Which statement is most appropriate?

A. The Beta model is suitable because \(Y\) is a binary success-or-failure variable.

B. The Beta model is suitable because \(Y\) is a continuous variable bounded between \(0\) and \(1\).

C. The Beta model is suitable because \(Y\) is a nonnegative count.

D. The Beta model is suitable because \(Y\) is continuous and unbounded.

Answer 2.4

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

The Beta distribution (see Section E.7) is a natural model for continuous random variables whose support lies between \(0\) and \(1\). It is not a binary distribution, a count distribution, or an unbounded continuous distribution.

Question 2.5

Open-ended Question

A logistics analyst models delivery time \(T\) in hours using a Gamma distribution with shape \(a>0\) and scale \(b>0\):

\[ f_T(t;a,b)=\frac{1}{\Gamma(a)b^a}t^{a-1}\exp\left(-\frac{t}{b}\right), \qquad t>0. \]

Assume

\[ \mathbb{E}(T)=ab \qquad \text{and} \qquad \operatorname{Var}(T)=ab^2. \]

A useful way to compare variability across positive continuous variables is the coefficient of variation. For a random variable with positive mean, it is defined as

\[ \operatorname{CV}(T) = \frac{\operatorname{SD}(T)}{\mathbb{E}(T)}. \]

Unlike the standard deviation \(\operatorname{SD}(T)\), which is measured in the same units as the variable, the coefficient of variation \(\operatorname{CV}(T)\) is unitless. It measures variability relative to the mean \(\mathbb{E}(T)\). For example, a standard deviation of \(2\) hours means something different when the mean delivery time is \(4\) hours than when the mean delivery time is \(40\) hours.

Derive the coefficient of variation for this Gamma model (see Section E.5). Then, explain why changing the shape parameter \(a\) affects relative variability.

Answer 2.5

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

Since

\[ \operatorname{SD}(T) = \sqrt{\operatorname{Var}(T)} = \sqrt{ab^2} = b\sqrt{a}, \]

we have

\[ \operatorname{CV}(T) = \frac{\operatorname{SD}(T)}{\mathbb{E}(T)} = \frac{b\sqrt{a}}{ab} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{a}}. \]

The scale parameter \(b\) cancels out, so the coefficient of variation is controlled only by the shape parameter \(a\). Larger values of \(a\) imply

\[ \operatorname{CV}(T)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{a}} \]

is smaller. In practical terms, this means delivery times become less variable relative to their mean.

This does not necessarily mean that the standard deviation itself is small. Since

\[ \operatorname{SD}(T)=b\sqrt{a}, \]

the absolute variability still depends on both \(a\) and \(b\). The coefficient of variation focuses specifically on relative variability, which is why it is useful when comparing systems with different average delivery times.

Question 2.6

True or False

If \(Y\sim\operatorname{Lognormal}(\eta,\sigma^2)\) (see Section E.3), then \(Y\) is continuous and positive, while \(\log(Y)\) is Normal with mean \(\eta\) and variance \(\sigma^2\) (see Section E.6).

Answer 2.6

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

The Lognormal distribution is commonly defined through the transformation

\[ \log(Y)\sim\operatorname{Normal}(\eta,\sigma^2). \]

Because \(Y=\exp\{\log(Y)\}\), the random variable \(Y\) is positive and continuous. This makes Lognormal models useful for right-skewed positive outcomes such as costs, times, and concentrations.

Question 2.7

Multiple Choice

A web platform records the number of purchases \(Y\) made by a user in a month. Many users make zero purchases, and the analyst suspects that some users are structurally inactive while others follow a Poisson purchasing process. Which distribution is most directly motivated by this description?

A. Normal

B. Zero-inflated Poisson

C. Beta

D. Logistic

Answer 2.7

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

A Zero-inflated Poisson model (see Section D.6) combines two mechanisms: one that generates extra zeros and another that generates counts from a Poisson distribution. This matches the description of structurally inactive users plus active users whose purchase counts may follow a Poisson-like process.

Question 2.8

Open-ended Question

A product team records the outcome of a user session as one of three categories: no purchase, one-time purchase, or subscription purchase. Let

\[ \mathbf{Y}=(Y_1,Y_2,Y_3)^{\top} \]

be the category-count vector among \(n\) independent sessions, with

\[ \mathbf{Y}\sim\operatorname{Multinomial}(n,\boldsymbol{\pi}), \qquad \boldsymbol{\pi}=(\pi_1,\pi_2,\pi_3)^\top, \]

where

\[ \pi_1+\pi_2+\pi_3=1 \]

and \(Y_k\) counts how many sessions fall in category \(k\) (see Section D.7).

Unlike a single Bernoulli trial, the multinomial model summarizes several session outcomes through a count vector. For example, if \(n=5\) and

\[ \mathbf{y}=(2,2,1)^{\top}, \]

then the count vector tells us that two sessions had no purchase, two had a one-time purchase, and one had a subscription purchase. However, it does not tell us the order in which those five outcomes occurred. Different session-level sequences can lead to the same final count vector.

Write the PMF of \(\mathbf{Y}\) and explain the role of the multinomial coefficient in that PMF.

Answer 2.8

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

For \(\mathbf{y}=(y_1,y_2,y_3)^{\top}\) such that \(y_k\in\{0,1,\ldots,n\}\) and \(y_1+y_2+y_3=n\), the PMF is

\[ p_{\mathbf{Y}}(\mathbf{y};\boldsymbol{\pi}) = \frac{n!}{y_1!y_2!y_3!} \pi_1^{y_1}\pi_2^{y_2}\pi_3^{y_3}. \]

The multinomial coefficient

\[ \frac{n!}{y_1!y_2!y_3!} \]

counts the number of distinct session-level sequences that produce the same category-count vector. It accounts for the fact that the counts do not record the order in which the session outcomes occurred.

Question 2.9

True or False

A Weibull distribution (see Section E.1) can be used for positive continuous time-to-event outcomes, and the Exponential distribution is a special case of the Weibull distribution for a particular shape parameter.

Hint: To justify your answer mathematically, write the Weibull PDF under a shape-scale parametrization and check what happens when the shape parameter is set equal to \(1\).

Answer 2.9

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

The Weibull distribution is commonly used for positive continuous time-to-event outcomes, such as failure times, survival times, delivery durations, or waiting times. Under a shape-scale parametrization, a Weibull random variable \(T\) with shape \(a>0\) and scale \(b>0\) has PDF

\[ f_T(t;a,b) = \frac{a}{b} \left(\frac{t}{b}\right)^{a-1} \exp\left[-\left(\frac{t}{b}\right)^a\right], \qquad t>0. \]

Now set the shape parameter equal to \(1\). Then

\[ f_T(t;1,b) = \frac{1}{b} \left(\frac{t}{b}\right)^{0} \exp\left[-\left(\frac{t}{b}\right)^1\right]. \]

Since

\[ \left(\frac{t}{b}\right)^0=1, \]

we obtain

\[ f_T(t;1,b) = \frac{1}{b} \exp\left(-\frac{t}{b}\right), \qquad t>0. \]

This is the PDF of an Exponential distribution with mean/scale parameter \(b\). Therefore, the Exponential distribution is a special case of the Weibull distribution when the Weibull shape parameter is

\[ a=1. \]

This relationship is helpful because the Weibull distribution extends the Exponential model by allowing different hazard shapes, while the Exponential model corresponds to the simpler special case with a constant hazard rate.

Question 2.10

Multiple Choice

Let \(Y\sim\operatorname{Negative Binomial}(k,\pi)\) (see Section D.3) denote the number of failures before observing \(k\) successes, where \(k\in\mathbb{N}\) and \(0<\pi<1\). Which support is appropriate?

A. \(y\in\{0,1,\ldots,k\}\)

B. \(y\in\{k,k+1,k+2,\ldots\}\)

C. \(y\in\{0,1,2,\ldots\}\)

D. \(0<y<1\)

Answer 2.10

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: C.

Rationale:

If \(Y\) counts the number of failures before observing \(k\) successes, then zero failures are possible, one failure is possible, and there is no fixed finite upper bound. Therefore,

\[ y\in\{0,1,2,\ldots\}. \]

Question 2.11

Open-ended Question

A quality-control analyst models a continuous measurement error \(Y\) using a Normal distribution with mean \(m\in\mathbb{R}\) and standard deviation \(s>0\) (see Section E.6):

\[ Y\sim \operatorname{Normal}(m,s^2). \]

The CDF can be written as

\[ F_Y(y;m,s) = \Phi\left(\frac{y-m}{s}\right), \qquad y\in\mathbb{R}, \]

where \(\Phi(\cdot)\) is the CDF of a standard Normal random variable. Show that the median of \(Y\) is \(m\).

Answer 2.11

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

A median \(q_{0.5}\) satisfies

\[ F_Y(q_{0.5};m,s)=0.5. \]

Using the Normal CDF,

\[ F_Y(q_{0.5};m,s) = \Phi\left(\frac{q_{0.5}-m}{s}\right). \]

We know that the standard Normal distribution is symmetric around \(0\), so

\[ \Phi(0)=0.5. \]

Therefore, to make

\[ \Phi\left(\frac{q_{0.5}-m}{s}\right)=0.5, \]

we need

\[ \frac{q_{0.5}-m}{s}=0. \]

Since \(s>0\), this implies

\[ q_{0.5}-m=0, \]

and hence

\[ q_{0.5}=m. \]

Therefore, the median of \(Y\) is \(m\). In this Normal model, the mean, median, and centre of symmetry are all equal to \(m\).

Question 2.12

True or False

For a continuous random variable \(Y\), the PDF value \(f_Y(y;\theta)\) is itself a probability, so it must always lie between \(0\) and \(1\).

Answer 2.12

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

For a continuous random variable, probabilities are obtained by integrating the PDF over intervals:

\[ \Pr(a\leq Y\leq b)=\int_a^b f_Y(y;\theta)\,\mathrm{d}y. \]

The density value \(f_Y(y;\theta)\) is not itself a probability and can exceed \(1\), as long as the total area under the density over the support is \(1\).

2.7.2 Random Sampling and Estimation

Question 2.13

Open-ended Question

A transportation analyst records the number of passenger arrivals per minute at a station. Assume

\[ Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda). \]

Let

\[ \bar{Y}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i. \]

Derive \(\mathbb{E}(\bar{Y})\) and \(\operatorname{Var}(\bar{Y})\). Then, interpret both results in context.

Answer 2.13

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

Since \(Y_i\sim\operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda)\) (see Section D.4),

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y_i)=\lambda, \qquad \operatorname{Var}(Y_i)=\lambda. \]

By linearity of expectation,

\[ \mathbb{E}(\bar{Y}) = \mathbb{E}\left(\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i\right) = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n\mathbb{E}(Y_i) = \lambda. \]

Using independence,

\[ \operatorname{Var}(\bar{Y}) = \operatorname{Var}\left(\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i\right) = \frac{1}{n^2}\sum_{i=1}^n\operatorname{Var}(Y_i) = \frac{\lambda}{n}. \]

Thus, the sample mean is unbiased for the arrival rate \(\lambda\), and its variance decreases as the number of observed minutes increases.

Question 2.14

Multiple Choice

A data scientist observes \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi)\) and uses

\[ \hat{\pi}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i. \]

Which statement is correct?

A. \(\hat{\pi}\) is a parameter because it is unknown before data collection.

B. \(\hat{\pi}\) is an estimator before the sample is observed and an estimate after the data are observed.

C. \(\hat{\pi}\) is the same as \(\pi\) for every possible random sample.

D. \(\hat{\pi}\) is not random because it is computed by a formula.

Answer 2.14

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

Before data are observed, \(\hat{\pi}\) is a random variable because it is a function of the random sample. After the observed data are plugged in, it becomes an observed estimate. The parameter \(\pi\) is fixed but unknown in the frequentist framework.

Question 2.15

True or False

If \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\) are independent and identically distributed, then their joint PMF or PDF factors into the product of the marginal PMFs or PDFs.

Answer 2.15

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

For an i.i.d. sample, independence gives the product factorization and identical distribution means the same PMF or PDF is used for each observation. Thus, for a generic parameter \(\theta\),

\[ p_{\mathbf{Y}}(\mathbf{y};\theta)=\prod_{i=1}^n p_Y(y_i;\theta) \]

in the discrete case, with the analogous expression using \(f_Y(y_i;\theta)\) in the continuous case.

Question 2.16

Open-ended Question

A reliability engineer observes lifetimes

\[ T_1,\ldots,T_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Exponential}(\mu), \]

where \(\mu>0\) is the mean/scale parameter. Assume

\[ \mathbb{E}(T_i)=\mu, \qquad \operatorname{Var}(T_i)=\mu^2. \]

Let \(\hat{\mu}=\bar{T}\). Derive \(\mathbb{E}(\hat{\mu})\), \(\operatorname{Var}(\hat{\mu})\), and the approximate sampling distribution of \(\hat{\mu}\) for large \(n\).

Answer 2.16

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

Since \(\hat{\mu}=\bar{T}\),

\[ \mathbb{E}(\hat{\mu}) = \mathbb{E}(\bar{T}) = \mu. \]

Also, by independence,

\[ \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\mu}) = \operatorname{Var}(\bar{T}) = \frac{\mu^2}{n}. \]

By the CLT,

\[ \frac{\hat{\mu}-\mu}{\mu/\sqrt{n}} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1), \]

or equivalently,

\[ \hat{\mu}\ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}\left(\mu,\frac{\mu^2}{n}\right) \]

for large \(n\).

Question 2.17

Multiple Choice

A study records the number of support tickets submitted by each customer in a month. A natural first model for this type of count outcome is a Poisson distribution:

\[ Y_i \sim \operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda), \]

where \(Y_i\) is the number of tickets submitted by customer \(i\). Under the classical Poisson model, the mean and variance are equal:

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y_i)=\lambda \qquad \text{and} \qquad \operatorname{Var}(Y_i)=\lambda. \]

Overdispersion occurs when the observed count outcome varies more than the Poisson model would suggest. In practice, this often means that the sample variance is noticeably larger than the sample mean. This can happen when customers differ substantially in their underlying ticket-submission rates, when there are unobserved groups of customers, or when some customers submit many more tickets than a simple Poisson model expects.

Which comparison is most directly relevant as a first descriptive check for overdispersion?

A. Whether the sample median is larger than the sample maximum.

B. Whether the sample variance is much larger than the sample mean.

C. Whether the sample mean is exactly zero.

D. Whether the sample size is odd or even.

Answer 2.17

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

For a classical Poisson model,

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y)=\operatorname{Var}(Y)=\lambda. \]

This equality is one of the strongest modelling restrictions of the Poisson distribution. Therefore, if the sample variance is much larger than the sample mean, the observed counts are more spread out than the classical Poisson model would lead us to expect.

For example, suppose the sample mean number of monthly support tickets is close to

\[ \bar{y}=2.1, \]

but the sample variance is

\[ s_y^2=9.4. \]
