The variance is more than four times the mean, which would be difficult to reconcile with a simple Poisson model unless the sample size is very small or the pattern is due to random sampling variation.

This descriptive check does not prove that any particular alternative model is correct. However, it is a warning sign that the Poisson mean-variance equality may be too restrictive. In later regression chapters, this motivates considering count models such as Negative Binomial, Generalized Poisson, or Zero-inflated Poisson.

Question 2.18

True or False

Suppose \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\) are i.i.d. observations from a population with finite mean \(\mu\) and finite variance \(\sigma^2\). The CLT says that, for a sufficiently large sample size \(n\), the sampling distribution of the sample mean \(\bar{Y}\) can often be approximated by

\[ \bar{Y} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}\left(\mu,\frac{\sigma^2}{n}\right). \]

True or false: This approximation means that the original population distribution of \(Y_i\) must also be Normal.

Answer 2.18

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

The CLT is about the sampling distribution of the sample mean, not necessarily about the shape of the original population distribution. Under suitable conditions, if

\[ Y_1,\ldots,Y_n \overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim} \text{some distribution with mean } \mu \text{ and variance } \sigma^2, \]

then the standardized sample mean satisfies the approximate relationship

\[ \frac{\bar{Y}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1) \]

for sufficiently large \(n\). Equivalently,

\[ \bar{Y} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}\left(\mu,\frac{\sigma^2}{n}\right). \]

This does not require the original observations \(Y_i\) to be normally distributed. They could come from many different distributions, such as a Gamma, Exponential, Bernoulli, Poisson, or another distribution with finite variance. What becomes approximately Normal is the sampling distribution of \(\bar{Y}\), not the original data distribution itself.

This is why the CLT is so useful for estimation and inference: it lets us use approximate Normal sampling distributions for estimators such as sample means and sample proportions, even when the original data are not Normal.

Question 2.19

Open-ended Question

A platform records whether each visitor activates a new feature. Let

\[ Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi), \qquad \hat{\pi}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i. \]

In estimation, the bias of an estimator measures whether the estimator is systematically too high or too low on average. For an estimator \(\hat{\theta}\) of a parameter \(\theta\), the bias is

\[ \operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\theta}) = \mathbb{E}(\hat{\theta})-\theta. \]

The mean squared error (MSE) measures the expected squared distance between the estimator and the parameter:

\[ \operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\theta}) = \mathbb{E}\left[(\hat{\theta}-\theta)^2\right]. \]

A useful identity is

\[ \operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\theta}) = \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\theta}) + \left[\operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\theta})\right]^2. \]

Derive \(\operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\pi})\) and \(\operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\pi})\).

Answer 2.19

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

First, compute the expected value of \(\hat{\pi}\):

\[ \mathbb{E}(\hat{\pi}) = \mathbb{E}\left(\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i\right). \]

By linearity of expectation,

\[ \mathbb{E}(\hat{\pi}) = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n\mathbb{E}(Y_i). \]

Since \(Y_i\sim\operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi)\),

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y_i)=\pi. \]

Therefore,

\[ \mathbb{E}(\hat{\pi}) = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n\pi = \frac{n\pi}{n} = \pi. \]

The bias is

\[ \operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\pi}) = \mathbb{E}(\hat{\pi})-\pi = \pi-\pi = 0. \]

Thus, \(\hat{\pi}\) is an unbiased estimator of \(\pi\).

Now compute the variance of \(\hat{\pi}\). Since

\[ \hat{\pi} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i, \]

we have

\[ \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\pi}) = \operatorname{Var}\left(\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i\right). \]

Using independence,

\[ \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\pi}) = \frac{1}{n^2}\sum_{i=1}^n\operatorname{Var}(Y_i). \]

Since \(Y_i\sim\operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi)\),

\[ \operatorname{Var}(Y_i)=\pi(1-\pi). \]

Therefore,

\[ \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\pi}) = \frac{1}{n^2}\sum_{i=1}^n\pi(1-\pi) = \frac{n\pi(1-\pi)}{n^2} = \frac{\pi(1-\pi)}{n}. \]

Using the bias-variance decomposition,

\[ \operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\pi}) = \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\pi}) + \left[\operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\pi})\right]^2. \]

Since \(\operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\pi})=0\),

\[ \operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\pi}) = \frac{\pi(1-\pi)}{n} + 0^2 = \frac{\pi(1-\pi)}{n}. \]

So,

\[ \boxed{ \operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\pi})=0 } \qquad \text{and} \qquad \boxed{ \operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\pi})=\frac{\pi(1-\pi)}{n}. } \]

The result shows that the estimator is centred correctly on \(\pi\), and its mean squared error decreases as the sample size \(n\) increases.

Question 2.20

Multiple Choice

A researcher observes \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Gamma}(a,b)\), where \(a\) and \(b\) are fixed unknown parameters (see Section E.5). Which statement best describes the observed sample \(\mathbf{y}=(y_1,\ldots,y_n)^{\top}\) after data collection?

A. \(\mathbf{y}\) is random because it came from a random process.

B. \(\mathbf{y}\) is fixed once observed, while the estimator before observation is random.

C. \(\mathbf{y}\) is the same as the parameter vector.

D. \(\mathbf{y}\) is a probability distribution.

Answer 2.20

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

Before data collection, the sample is represented by random variables. After data collection, the realized values are fixed observed data. Estimators are functions of random variables before observation and become estimates after the observed data are plugged in.

Question 2.21

True or False

The sample mean of i.i.d. Lognormal observations (see Section E.3) is guaranteed to have an exactly Normal (see Section E.6) sampling distribution for every sample size \(n\).

Answer 2.21

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

Lognormal observations are not Normal on the original scale. The CLT may justify an approximate Normal sampling distribution for the sample mean when the sample size \(n\) is sufficiently large and the variance is finite, but the distribution is not exactly Normal for every sample size.

Question 2.22

Open-ended Question

A biomedical researcher records \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Beta}(\alpha,\beta)\) (see Section E.7), where

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y_i)=\frac{\alpha}{\alpha+\beta}, \qquad \operatorname{Var}(Y_i)=\frac{\alpha\beta}{(\alpha+\beta)^2(\alpha+\beta+1)}. \]

Let \(\bar{Y}=n^{-1}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i\). Derive the large-sample approximate distribution of \(\bar{Y}\).

Answer 2.22

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

By the CLT,

\[ \frac{\bar{Y}-\mathbb{E}(Y_i)}{\sqrt{\operatorname{Var}(Y_i)/n}} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1). \]

Substituting the Beta mean and variance gives

\[ \bar{Y} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}\left( \frac{\alpha}{\alpha+\beta}, \frac{1}{n}\frac{\alpha\beta}{(\alpha+\beta)^2(\alpha+\beta+1)} \right). \]

This approximation becomes more useful as \(n\) increases, provided the sample is reasonably treated as i.i.d. from the Beta model.

Question 2.23

Multiple Choice

Suppose \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\) are i.i.d. with finite mean \(\mu\) and finite variance \(\sigma^2\). Which expression is the CLT-based pivot for the sample mean when \(\sigma\) is known?

A. \(\displaystyle \frac{\bar{Y}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1)\)

B. \(\displaystyle \frac{\bar{Y}-\sigma}{\mu/\sqrt{n}}\ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1)\)

C. \(\displaystyle \frac{Y_i-\bar{Y}}{n}\ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1)\)

D. \(\displaystyle \frac{\mu-\sigma}{\bar{Y}}\ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1)\)

Answer 2.23

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: A.

Rationale:

The CLT standardizes the sample mean by subtracting its mean and dividing by its standard error:

\[ \frac{\bar{Y}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}} \ \dot{\sim}\ \operatorname{Normal}(0,1). \]

The denominator is the standard deviation of the sample mean, not the standard deviation of a single observation.

Question 2.24

True or False

An estimator can be biased and still have a smaller MSE than an unbiased estimator.

Answer 2.24

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

MSE decomposes as

\[ \operatorname{MSE}(\hat{\theta}) = \operatorname{Var}(\hat{\theta})+ [\operatorname{Bias}(\hat{\theta})\bigr]^2. \]

A biased estimator can have lower variance by enough to offset its squared bias, yielding smaller MSE than an unbiased estimator. This is a central bias-variance tradeoff idea.

2.7.3 Maximum Likelihood Estimation

Question 2.25

Open-ended Question

A quality team observes \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda)\) (see Section D.4). Derive the maximum likelihood estimator of \(\lambda\).

Answer 2.25

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

With \(\mathbf{y}=(y_1,\ldots,y_n)^{\top}\), the likelihood is

\[ \mathcal{L}(\lambda;\mathbf{y}) = \prod_{i=1}^n\frac{\exp(-\lambda)\lambda^{y_i}}{y_i!} = \exp(-n\lambda)\lambda^{\sum_{i=1}^ny_i}\prod_{i=1}^n\frac{1}{y_i!}. \]

The log-likelihood is

\[ \ell(\lambda;\mathbf{y}) = -n\lambda+\left(\sum_{i=1}^ny_i\right)\log(\lambda)-\sum_{i=1}^n\log(y_i!). \]

Differentiate:

\[ \frac{\partial \ell(\lambda;\mathbf{y})}{\partial \lambda} = -n+\frac{\sum_{i=1}^ny_i}{\lambda}. \]

Set equal to zero:

\[ -n+\frac{\sum_{i=1}^ny_i}{\lambda}=0 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \bar{Y}. \]

The second derivative is

\[ \frac{\partial^2 \ell(\lambda;\mathbf{y})}{\partial \lambda^2} = -\frac{\sum_{i=1}^ny_i}{\lambda^2}<0 \]

whenever at least one event is observed, confirming a maximum.

Question 2.26

Multiple Choice

A researcher observes \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi)\). With \(\mathbf{y}=(y_1,\ldots,y_n)^{\top}\), which likelihood is correct?

A. \(\mathcal{L}(\pi;\mathbf{y})=\prod_{i=1}^n\pi^{y_i}(1-\pi)^{1-y_i}\)

B. \(\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{y};\pi)=\prod_{i=1}^n\pi^{\pi}(1-y_i)^{1-\pi}\)

C. \(\mathcal{L}(\pi;\mathbf{y})=\sum_{i=1}^n\pi^{y_i}(1-\pi)^{1-y_i}\)

D. \(\mathcal{L}(\pi;\mathbf{y})=\pi(1-\pi)\)

Answer 2.26

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: A.

Rationale:

For i.i.d. Bernoulli observations, the likelihood is the product of the PMFs evaluated at the observed data:

\[ \mathcal{L}(\pi;\mathbf{y}) = \prod_{i=1}^n\pi^{y_i}(1-\pi)^{1-y_i}. \]

Option A also follows the chapter convention of writing the parameter before the semicolon and the observed data after it in likelihood notation.

Question 2.27

True or False

In frequentist maximum likelihood estimation, it is more precise to say that the MLE is the parameter value most compatible with the observed data under the model, rather than saying the parameter value is the most probable.

Answer 2.27

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

In the frequentist framework, the parameter is fixed but unknown. The likelihood is a function of the parameter after the data are observed, but it is not a probability distribution over the parameter. Therefore, saying that the MLE is most compatible with the observed data under the model is more precise.

Question 2.28

Open-ended Question

A risk analyst models large insurance claim amounts using a Pareto distribution (see Section E.2). Let

\[ Y_1,\ldots,Y_n \overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim} \operatorname{Pareto}(y_{\min}, a), \]

where \(y_{\min}>0\) is a known minimum claim threshold and \(a>0\) is an unknown shape parameter. The PDF is

\[ f_Y(y;a) = \frac{a y_{\min}^{a}}{y^{a+1}}, \qquad y\geq y_{\min}. \]

The Pareto distribution is often used for positive, heavy-tailed outcomes. In this parametrization, smaller values of \(a\) correspond to heavier tails, meaning that very large claims become more plausible.

Assume \(y_{\min}\) is known. Derive the MLE of the shape parameter \(a\).

Answer 2.28

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

The likelihood is

\[ \mathcal{L}(a;\mathbf{y}) = \prod_{i=1}^n \frac{a y_{\min}^{a}}{y_i^{a+1}}, \qquad y_i\geq y_{\min}. \]

We can separate the terms involving \(a\):

\[ \mathcal{L}(a;\mathbf{y}) = a^n y_{\min}^{an} \prod_{i=1}^n y_i^{-(a+1)}. \]

Taking logs gives the log-likelihood

\[ \ell(a;\mathbf{y}) = n\log(a) + an\log(y_{\min}) - (a+1)\sum_{i=1}^n \log(y_i). \]

Now differentiate with respect to \(a\):

\[ \frac{\partial \ell(a;\mathbf{y})}{\partial a} = \frac{n}{a} + n\log(y_{\min}) - \sum_{i=1}^n \log(y_i). \]

Set the score equal to zero:

\[ \frac{n}{a} + n\log(y_{\min}) - \sum_{i=1}^n \log(y_i) = 0. \]

Rearranging,

\[ \frac{n}{a} = \sum_{i=1}^n \log(y_i) - n\log(y_{\min}). \]

Since

\[ \sum_{i=1}^n \log(y_i) - n\log(y_{\min}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \log\left(\frac{y_i}{y_{\min}}\right), \]

we obtain

\[ \frac{n}{a} = \sum_{i=1}^n \log\left(\frac{y_i}{y_{\min}}\right). \]

Therefore, the MLE is

\[ \hat{a}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{n}{ \sum_{i=1}^n \log\left(\frac{Y_i}{y_{\min}}\right) }. \]

To check that this critical point is a maximum, compute the second derivative:

\[ \frac{\partial^2 \ell(a;\mathbf{y})}{\partial a^2} = -\frac{n}{a^2}. \]

Since \(a>0\),

\[ -\frac{n}{a^2}<0, \]

so the log-likelihood is concave in \(a\) and the critical point is the maximum.

Thus,

\[ \boxed{ \hat{a}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{n}{ \sum_{i=1}^n \log\left(\frac{Y_i}{y_{\min}}\right) } }. \]

In practical terms, if many claims are far above \(y_{\min}\), then the observed logarithmic ratios \(\log(y_i/y_{\min})\) tend to be larger, which makes \(\hat{a}_{\operatorname{MLE}}\) smaller. This corresponds to a heavier fitted tail. If claims are mostly close to \(y_{\min}\), then the logarithmic ratios tend to be smaller, which makes \(\hat{a}_{\operatorname{MLE}}\) larger and indicates a lighter fitted tail.

Question 2.29

Multiple Choice

A data scientist fits a Normal model

\[ Y_i \overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim} \operatorname{Normal}(\mu,\sigma^2), \]

with both \(\mu\) and \(\sigma^2\) unknown (see Section E.6). Which estimator is the MLE of \(\sigma^2\)?

A. \(\displaystyle \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n(Y_i-\bar{Y})^2\)

B. \(\displaystyle \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{1}{n-1}\sum_{i=1}^n(Y_i-\bar{Y})^2\)

C. \(\displaystyle \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^nY_i^2\)

D. \(\displaystyle \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\bar{Y}\)

Answer 2.29

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: A.

Rationale:

For the Normal model,

\[ Y_i \overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim} \operatorname{Normal}(\mu,\sigma^2), \]

the PDF of one observation is

\[ f_Y(y_i;\mu,\sigma^2) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}} \exp\left[ -\frac{(y_i-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2} \right], \qquad -\infty<y_i<\infty. \]

For an observed sample \(\mathbf{y}=(y_1,\ldots,y_n)^\top\), the likelihood is

\[ \mathcal{L}(\mu,\sigma^2;\mathbf{y}) = \prod_{i=1}^n \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}} \exp\left[ -\frac{(y_i-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2} \right]. \]

Combining terms,

\[ \mathcal{L}(\mu,\sigma^2;\mathbf{y}) = (2\pi\sigma^2)^{-n/2} \exp\left[ -\frac{1}{2\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2 \right]. \]

The log-likelihood is

\[ \ell(\mu,\sigma^2;\mathbf{y}) = -\frac{n}{2}\log(2\pi) -\frac{n}{2}\log(\sigma^2) -\frac{1}{2\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2. \]

First, maximize with respect to \(\mu\). Differentiate:

\[ \frac{\partial \ell(\mu,\sigma^2;\mathbf{y})}{\partial \mu} = \frac{1}{\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i-\mu). \]

Set the score equal to zero:

\[ \frac{1}{\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i-\mu) = 0. \]

Since \(\sigma^2>0\), this is equivalent to

\[ \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i-\mu)=0. \]

Then

\[ \sum_{i=1}^n y_i - n\mu = 0, \]

so

\[ \hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n y_i = \bar{y}. \]

Now maximize with respect to \(\sigma^2\). Let

\[ v=\sigma^2. \]

Then the log-likelihood can be written as

\[ \ell(\mu,v;\mathbf{y}) = -\frac{n}{2}\log(2\pi) -\frac{n}{2}\log(v) -\frac{1}{2v} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2, \qquad v>0. \]

Differentiate with respect to \(v\):

\[ \frac{\partial \ell(\mu,v;\mathbf{y})}{\partial v} = -\frac{n}{2v} + \frac{1}{2v^2} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2. \]

Set the score equal to zero:

\[ -\frac{n}{2v} + \frac{1}{2v^2} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2 = 0. \]

Multiply both sides by \(2v^2\):

\[ -nv + \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2 = 0. \]

Therefore,

\[ v = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\mu)^2. \]

Since the MLE of \(\mu\) is \(\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\bar{y}\), the MLE of \(\sigma^2\) is

\[ \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i-\bar{y})^2. \]

In random-variable notation, this corresponds to

\[ \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n (Y_i-\bar{Y})^2. \]

Thus, the correct answer is

\[ \boxed{ \hat{\sigma}^2_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n (Y_i-\bar{Y})^2 }. \]

The estimator with denominator \(n-1\),

\[ \frac{1}{n-1} \sum_{i=1}^n (Y_i-\bar{Y})^2, \]

is the usual unbiased sample variance. It corrects the bias introduced by estimating \(\mu\) with \(\bar{Y}\), but it is not the MLE. The MLE is obtained by directly maximizing the Normal likelihood, and that optimization gives denominator \(n\).

Question 2.30

True or False

A hospital operations team models the number of patient arrivals to an urgent-care unit in one hour as

\[ Y_i \overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim} \operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda), \]

where \(\lambda>0\) is the average number of arrivals per hour. Suppose the MLE of \(\lambda\) is

\[ \hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \bar{Y}. \]

The operations team is not only interested in the arrival rate \(\lambda\). They also want to estimate the probability that at least one patient arrives in a future one-hour period. Under the Poisson model, this probability is

\[ g(\lambda) = \Pr(Y\geq 1) = 1-\Pr(Y=0) = 1-\exp(-\lambda). \]

True or false: By the invariance property of MLEs, the MLE of \(g(\lambda)=1-\exp(-\lambda)\) is

\[ g(\hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}}) = 1-\exp(-\hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}}) = 1-\exp(-\bar{Y}). \]

Answer 2.30

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

The invariance property of MLEs says that if \(\hat{\theta}_{\operatorname{MLE}}\) is the MLE of \(\theta\), then, for a suitable transformation \(g(\cdot)\), the MLE of \(g(\theta)\) is

\[ g(\hat{\theta}_{\operatorname{MLE}}). \]

In this problem, the original model parameter is the Poisson rate \(\lambda\), and the MLE is

\[ \hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \bar{Y}. \]

The stakeholder-facing quantity is not \(\lambda\) itself, but the probability of at least one arrival in a future hour:

\[ g(\lambda) = \Pr(Y\geq 1). \]

Using the Poisson PMF,

\[ p_Y(y;\lambda) = \frac{\lambda^y\exp(-\lambda)}{y!}, \qquad y=0,1,2,\ldots, \]

we have

\[ \Pr(Y=0) = p_Y(0;\lambda) = \frac{\lambda^0\exp(-\lambda)}{0!} = \exp(-\lambda). \]

Therefore,

\[ \Pr(Y\geq 1) = 1-\Pr(Y=0) = 1-\exp(-\lambda). \]

Thus, the quantity of interest is

\[ g(\lambda)=1-\exp(-\lambda). \]

By the invariance property, the MLE of this probability is obtained by replacing \(\lambda\) with its MLE:

\[ \widehat{g(\lambda)}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = g(\hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}}) = 1-\exp(-\hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}}). \]

Since

\[ \hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\bar{Y}, \]

we get

\[ \widehat{\Pr(Y\geq 1)}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = 1-\exp(-\bar{Y}). \]

So the statement is true. In practical terms, once the hospital estimates the average hourly arrival rate, it can use the fitted Poisson model to estimate the probability of seeing at least one arrival in a future hour.

Question 2.31

Open-ended Question

A risk team observes \(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Bernoulli}(\pi)\). Derive the MLE of the odds

\[ \omega=\frac{\pi}{1-\pi}. \]

Assume \(0<\sum_{i=1}^n y_i<n\).

Answer 2.31

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

For i.i.d. Bernoulli observations, the MLE of \(\pi\) is

\[ \hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\bar{Y}. \]

The odds are a transformation of \(\pi\):

\[ \omega=g(\pi)=\frac{\pi}{1-\pi}. \]

By the invariance property of MLEs,

\[ \hat{\omega}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = g(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE}}) = \frac{\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE}}}{1-\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE}}} = \frac{\bar{Y}}{1-\bar{Y}}. \]

Equivalently, if \(s=\sum_{i=1}^ny_i\), then

\[ \hat{\omega}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{s/n}{1-s/n} = \frac{s}{n-s}. \]

Question 2.32

Multiple Choice

A sample \(y_1,\ldots,y_n\) comes from a Lognormal model (see Section E.3), where

\[ \log(Y_i)\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Normal}(\eta,\sigma^2). \]

Here, \(Y_i\) is positive and right-skewed on its original scale, but \(\log(Y_i)\) is modelled as Normal. If \(\sigma^2\) is known, which expression is the MLE of \(\eta\)?

A. \(\displaystyle \hat{\eta}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n Y_i\)

B. \(\displaystyle \hat{\eta}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \log(Y_i)\)

C. \(\displaystyle \hat{\eta}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\log\left(\sum_{i=1}^nY_i\right)\)

D. \(\displaystyle \hat{\eta}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{1}{n-1}\sum_{i=1}^n\log(Y_i)\)

Answer 2.32

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

The key point is that the Normal model is placed on the log-transformed observations, not on the original observations. Define

\[ X_i=\log(Y_i). \]

Then the model statement

\[ \log(Y_i)\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Normal}(\eta,\sigma^2) \]

can be rewritten as

\[ X_i\overset{\operatorname{i.i.d.}}{\sim}\operatorname{Normal}(\eta,\sigma^2), \]

where \(\eta\) is the mean of the log-transformed variable \(X_i\).

Since \(\sigma^2\) is known, the likelihood for \(\eta\) based on the transformed observations \(x_i=\log(y_i)\) is proportional to

\[ \mathcal{L}(\eta;\mathbf{x}) \propto \prod_{i=1}^n \exp\left[ -\frac{(x_i-\eta)^2}{2\sigma^2} \right]. \]

Taking logs and dropping constants that do not depend on \(\eta\) gives

\[ \ell(\eta;\mathbf{x}) = -\frac{1}{2\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n (x_i-\eta)^2 + \text{constant}. \]

Thus, maximizing the log-likelihood is equivalent to minimizing

\[ \sum_{i=1}^n (x_i-\eta)^2. \]

Differentiate the log-likelihood with respect to \(\eta\):

\[ \frac{\partial \ell(\eta;\mathbf{x})}{\partial \eta} = \frac{1}{\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n(x_i-\eta). \]

Set the score equal to zero:

\[ \frac{1}{\sigma^2} \sum_{i=1}^n(x_i-\eta) = 0. \]

Since \(\sigma^2>0\), this is equivalent to

\[ \sum_{i=1}^n x_i - n\eta = 0. \]

Therefore,

\[ \hat{\eta}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n X_i. \]

Substituting back \(X_i=\log(Y_i)\) gives

\[ \hat{\eta}_{\operatorname{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \log(Y_i). \]

So the correct answer is B.

Option A averages the observations on the original scale, but \(\eta\) is the mean on the log scale. Option C takes the log after summing, which is not the same as averaging log-values. Option D uses denominator \(n-1\), which is not the MLE for a Normal mean.

Question 2.33

True or False

In maximum likelihood estimation, the goal is to find the parameter value that maximizes the likelihood function. With \(\mathbf{y}=(y_1,\ldots,y_n)^{\top}\) and parameter \(\theta\), suppose two likelihood functions are related by

\[ \mathcal{L}_2(\theta;\mathbf{y}) = c\,\mathcal{L}_1(\theta;\mathbf{y}), \]

where \(c>0\) is a constant that does not depend on \(\theta\).

True or false: Multiplying the likelihood by this positive constant changes the location of the MLE.

Answer 2.33

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

The MLE is the parameter value that maximizes the likelihood. If

\[ \mathcal{L}_2(\theta;\mathbf{y}) = c\,\mathcal{L}_1(\theta;\mathbf{y}), \]

where \(c>0\) does not depend on \(\theta\), then \(\mathcal{L}_2(\theta;\mathbf{y})\) is just a vertically rescaled version of \(\mathcal{L}_1(\theta;\mathbf{y})\). The height of the likelihood changes, but the parameter value where the maximum occurs does not.

Mathematically, for any two candidate parameter values \(\theta_a\) and \(\theta_b\),

\[ \mathcal{L}_1(\theta_a;\mathbf{y}) > \mathcal{L}_1(\theta_b;\mathbf{y}) \]

implies

\[ c\,\mathcal{L}_1(\theta_a;\mathbf{y}) > c\,\mathcal{L}_1(\theta_b;\mathbf{y}), \]

because \(c>0\). Therefore, multiplying by \(c\) preserves the ordering of likelihood values across parameter values.

Equivalently,

\[ \operatorname*{arg\,max}_{\theta}\mathcal{L}_2(\theta;\mathbf{y}) = \operatorname*{arg\,max}_{\theta}c\,\mathcal{L}_1(\theta;\mathbf{y}) = \operatorname*{arg\,max}_{\theta}\mathcal{L}_1(\theta;\mathbf{y}). \]

This is also why analysts often work with the log-likelihood and drop additive constants that do not depend on the parameter. Taking logs gives

\[ \log\mathcal{L}_2(\theta;\mathbf{y}) = \log c + \log\mathcal{L}_1(\theta;\mathbf{y}). \]

The term \(\log c\) shifts the log-likelihood up or down by a constant amount, but it does not change the value of \(\theta\) that maximizes it.

So, multiplying the likelihood by a positive parameter-free constant changes the scale of the likelihood, not the location of the MLE.

Question 2.34

Open-ended Question

A conversion experiment observes \(Y\sim\operatorname{Binomial}(n,\pi)\), where \(Y\) is the number of conversions among \(n\) independent visitors. The PMF is

\[ p_Y(y;n,\pi)=\binom{n}{y}\pi^y(1-\pi)^{n-y}, \qquad y\in\{0,1,\ldots,n\}. \]

Derive the MLE of \(\pi\) from the Binomial likelihood.

Answer 2.34

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

The likelihood is

\[ \mathcal{L}(\pi;y) = \binom{n}{y}\pi^y(1-\pi)^{n-y}. \]

The log-likelihood is

\[ \ell(\pi;y) = \log\binom{n}{y}+y\log(\pi)+(n-y)\log(1-\pi). \]

Differentiate with respect to \(\pi\):

\[ \frac{\partial \ell(\pi;y)}{\partial \pi} = \frac{y}{\pi}-\frac{n-y}{1-\pi}. \]

Set equal to zero:

\[ \frac{y}{\pi}=\frac{n-y}{1-\pi}. \]

Cross-multiplying gives

\[ y(1-\pi)=\pi(n-y) \quad\Longrightarrow\quad y= n\pi. \]

Thus,

\[ \hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE}}=\frac{Y}{n}. \]

For the observed data, the estimate is \(\hat{\pi}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}=y/n\).

Question 2.35

Multiple Choice

Suppose a numerical optimizer returns the value of \(\theta\) that minimizes the negative log-likelihood

\[ -\ell(\theta;\mathbf{y}). \]

What has the optimizer found?

A. The least-squares estimate regardless of the probability model.

B. The maximum likelihood estimate, if the optimizer successfully found the global minimum.

C. The posterior mean.

D. The sampling distribution of \(\hat{\theta}\).

Answer 2.35

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

Maximizing the log-likelihood is equivalent to minimizing the negative log-likelihood. Therefore, a successful global minimization of \(-\ell(\theta;\mathbf{y})\) returns the maximum likelihood estimate.

Question 2.36

True or False

The likelihood function \(\mathcal{L}(\boldsymbol{\theta};\mathbf{y})\) is constructed after the data are observed by treating \(\mathbf{y}\) as fixed and the parameter vector \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) as the argument of the function.

Answer 2.36

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: True.

Rationale:

This is the core likelihood perspective. The probability model describes random data before observation. After observing \(\mathbf{y}\), the likelihood views the observed data as fixed and studies how compatible different parameter values are with those data under the model.

2.7.4 Goodness of Fit: Does the Fitted Probability Model Look Adequate?

Question 2.37

Multiple Choice

A website analyst models the number of page views per session using a Poisson model:

\[ Y_i \sim \operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda), \]

where \(Y_i\) is the number of page views in session \(i\). The Poisson distribution is a natural starting point for count data because it is defined on

\[ y=0,1,2,\ldots. \]

However, the classical Poisson model also imposes an important mean-variance restriction:

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y_i)=\lambda \qquad \text{and} \qquad \operatorname{Var}(Y_i)=\lambda. \]

This property is often called equidispersion: the mean and variance are equal under the model. Therefore, as a first descriptive goodness-of-fit check, an analyst can compare the observed sample mean and observed sample variance. In this study, the observed sample mean is

\[ \bar{y}=2.1, \]

while the observed sample variance is

\[ s_y^2=9.8. \]

Which conclusion is most appropriate as a first goodness-of-fit concern?

A. The Poisson model is automatically correct because the response is a count.

B. The variance is much larger than the mean, so overdispersion is a concern.

C. The response must be continuous because the variance is not an integer.

D. The sample mean and sample variance are irrelevant for checking a Poisson model.

Answer 2.37

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

For a Poisson distribution,

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y)=\operatorname{Var}(Y)=\lambda. \]

This means that the same parameter \(\lambda\) controls both the centre and the spread of the distribution. If a fitted Poisson model has an estimated mean around

\[ \hat{\lambda}\approx \bar{y}=2.1, \]

then the model also expects the variance to be roughly around

\[ 2.1. \]

However, the observed sample variance is

\[ s_y^2=9.8, \]

which is much larger than the sample mean. The ratio

\[ \frac{s_y^2}{\bar{y}} = \frac{9.8}{2.1} \approx 4.67 \]

suggests that the observed counts are far more variable than a simple Poisson model would expect.

This pattern is called overdispersion. It does not prove that the Poisson model is wrong in every possible sense, and it does not identify one unique replacement model by itself. However, it is a strong first goodness-of-fit warning that the Poisson equidispersion assumption may be too restrictive for these data.

In this applied setting, overdispersion could arise because some sessions are very short while others involve unusually heavy browsing, because users come from different behavioural groups, or because important predictors such as traffic source, device type, or user intent have not been included. In later count-regression chapters, this kind of pattern motivates alternatives such as Negative Binomial, Generalized Poisson, or Zero-inflated Poisson/

Therefore, the most appropriate answer is B.

Question 2.38

True or False

A support team fits a Poisson model to daily ticket counts so that it can summarize the typical volume of incoming requests and plan staffing. After fitting the model, the analyst compares the observed ticket-count pattern with the counts expected under the fitted Poisson distribution.

True or false: If the goodness-of-fit checks do not show a major departure from the fitted Poisson model, this means the Poisson model has been proven to be the true data-generating mechanism for daily ticket counts.

Answer 2.38

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

Goodness of fit asks whether the fitted model is adequate for the observed data and the modelling inquiry. It does not prove that the fitted model is literally true.

In this support-ticket setting, a Poisson model might be useful if it captures the main pattern in daily ticket counts well enough for planning staffing levels. However, even if the observed counts look reasonably consistent with the fitted Poisson distribution, there may still be features the model does not capture, such as day-of-week patterns, holiday effects, product-release spikes, dependence across days, or differences between ordinary and high-volume support periods.

Thus, a goodness-of-fit check can provide evidence that a model is reasonable for a specific purpose, and it can also reveal signs of model inadequacy. But it cannot prove that the model is the true mechanism that generated the data. In applied work, the more realistic question is whether the model is useful and adequate enough for the decision being made.

Question 2.39

Open-ended Question

A manufacturing team measures the diameter error \(Y_i\) of machine-produced parts, where \(Y_i=0\) means the part is exactly on target, positive values mean the part is too large, and negative values mean the part is too small. An analyst fits a Normal model,

\[ Y_i \sim \operatorname{Normal}(\mu,\sigma^2), \]

and obtains the fitted values

\[ \hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}=0.02 \qquad \text{and} \qquad \hat{\sigma}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}=0.10. \]

The analyst then standardizes the observed errors using

\[ z_i = \frac{y_i-\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}}{\hat{\sigma}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}}. \]

Figure 2.21 shows an illustrative histogram of standardized errors from this fitted model.

Under a reasonably fitting Normal model, most standardized values should lie between about \(-2\) and \(2\), and only a small number should be far outside that range. However, the histogram shows several large positive standardized errors and a heavier right tail than a Normal model would suggest.

Explain what this pattern suggests about the goodness of fit of the Normal model. What practical modelling concerns should the manufacturing team consider before using this fitted model for quality-control decisions?

Answer 2.39

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

The standardized values

\[ z_i = \frac{y_i-\hat{\mu}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}}{\hat{\sigma}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}} \]

put the observed errors on a common scale relative to the fitted Normal model. If the fitted Normal model is adequate, then these standardized values should behave roughly like observations from a standard Normal distribution:

\[ Z_i \sim \operatorname{Normal}(0,1). \]

Under a standard Normal distribution, values larger than \(3\) in absolute value are unusual. In particular,

\[ \Pr(|Z|>3) = \Pr(Z<-3)+\Pr(Z>3) \approx 0.0027. \]

This means that, under a well-fitting Normal model, only about \(0.27\%\) of observations would be expected to fall more than three standard deviations from the fitted mean. Therefore, seeing several standardized values with

\[ |z_i|>3 \]

is a warning sign that the fitted Normal model may not describe the tails of the observed data well.

The heavy right tail, in Figure 2.21, is especially important. It suggests that unusually large positive diameter errors occur more often than the Normal model would expect. In the manufacturing context, this could mean that the process sometimes produces parts that are much too large, perhaps because of machine drift, tool wear, calibration problems, material differences, or unobserved production conditions.

As a goodness-of-fit conclusion, this does not prove that the Normal model is useless or that one specific alternative model must be correct. However, it does suggest that the Normal model may be inadequate for quality-control decisions that depend on tail behaviour. If the team uses the fitted Normal model to estimate the probability of extreme diameter errors, the model may underestimate the risk of producing parts that are far too large.

Before relying on this model, the team should investigate the large positive errors, check whether they correspond to specific machines or production periods, and consider whether a different model, transformation, mixture structure, or stratified analysis is needed. The key point is that goodness of fit should be assessed relative to the decision: if the decision depends on extreme production errors, then poor tail fit is a serious modelling concern.

Question 2.40

Multiple Choice

A data scientist fits a Lognormal model to positive insurance claim amounts. Which plot is most directly aligned with checking whether the fitted distribution captures the observed marginal shape of the response?

A. A histogram of claim amounts with the fitted Lognormal density overlaid.

B. A bar plot of randomly assigned observation IDs.

C. A scatterplot of claim amount against row number only, with no model overlay.

D. A pie chart of the fitted parameter values.

Answer 2.40

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: A.

Rationale:

A histogram with the fitted density overlaid is a direct visual check of whether the fitted continuous distribution follows the main shape of the observed data. It can reveal skewness, tails, or multi-modality not captured by the fitted model.

Question 2.41

True or False

A subscription platform models the number of failed payment attempts per customer in a month using a Poisson distribution. The analyst estimates the Poisson mean by maximum likelihood and obtains

\[ \hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}=0.80. \]

The product team then says:

“Since we used MLE, the fitted Poisson model must be the correct probability model for failed payment attempts. We do not need any goodness-of-fit checks.”

True or false: The product team’s statement is correct.

Answer 2.41

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: False.

Rationale:

Maximum likelihood estimation estimates parameters within a chosen model family. In this case, MLE finds the value of \(\lambda\) that makes the observed failed-payment counts most compatible with a Poisson model:

\[ Y_i \sim \operatorname{Poisson}(\lambda). \]

For a Poisson model, the mean and variance are tied together:

\[ \mathbb{E}(Y_i)=\lambda \qquad \text{and} \qquad \operatorname{Var}(Y_i)=\lambda. \]

Therefore, estimating

\[ \hat{\lambda}_{\operatorname{MLE,obs}}=0.80 \]

does not prove that failed payment attempts truly follow a Poisson distribution. It only gives the best-fitting Poisson mean under the assumption that the Poisson model is the chosen model family.

Goodness-of-fit checks are still needed because the observed data may show patterns that the fitted Poisson model cannot capture. For example, the data might have more zeroes than expected if many customers never experience payment problems. They might also be overdispersed if a small group of customers has repeated failed attempts, making the variance much larger than the mean. There may also be customer-level differences, billing-cycle effects, or payment-method effects that a simple Poisson model ignores.

Thus, MLE and goodness of fit answer different questions. MLE asks:

\[ \text{Which parameter value fits best within this model family?} \]

Goodness of fit asks:

\[ \text{Is this fitted model family adequate for the observed data and the modelling inquiry?} \]

The product team’s statement is therefore false. MLE does not remove the need for goodness-of-fit checks.

Question 2.42

Open-ended Question

A pharmacy records the number of prescription refills per customer over a fixed follow-up period. Let \(Y_i\) be the refill count for customer \(i\), so

\[ Y_i=0,1,2,\ldots. \]

A simple Poisson model may be a natural first model for count data, but the analyst notices that many customers have zero refills. Some of these zeroes may come from ordinary Poisson variation, while others may come from customers who are not at risk of refilling during the period, such as customers who discontinued the medication or only needed a one-time prescription.

To account for this, the analyst considers a zero-inflated Poisson model. Informally, this model adds an extra mechanism for producing zeroes, beyond the zeroes expected from a standard Poisson distribution. The goal is not only to estimate the model parameters, but also to check whether the fitted model captures the main features of the observed refill-count distribution.

Describe two goodness-of-fit checks that would be more informative than only reporting the fitted parameters.

Answer 2.42

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

One useful check is to compare the observed number or proportion of zero refill counts with the fitted expected number or proportion of zeroes under the zero-inflated Poisson model. Since the model was introduced specifically because zeroes are unusually common, the fitted model should reproduce the zero-count pattern reasonably well. If the fitted model still predicts far fewer zeroes than observed, then it may not have adequately captured the excess-zero structure. If it predicts too many zeroes, it may be overcorrecting.

A second useful check is to compare the observed and fitted frequencies across several count categories, such as

\[ 0,\ 1,\ 2,\ 3,\ \text{and }4\text{ or more refills}. \]

This matters because a model can fit the number of zeroes reasonably well but still fit the positive counts poorly. For example, the fitted model might reproduce the observed number of zero-refill customers but underestimate the number of customers with many refills. That would suggest that the positive-count part of the model is not capturing the tail behaviour or heterogeneity in refill patterns.

Together, these checks ask whether the fitted model captures both key parts of the data: the excess-zero behaviour and the distribution of nonzero refill counts. Reporting only the fitted parameters would not show whether the model actually reproduces the observed count pattern well enough for the pharmacy’s modelling inquiry.

Question 2.43

Multiple Choice

A fitted Gamma model (see Section E.5) for positive service times has a fitted mean near the sample mean, but the histogram shows two distinct peaks corresponding to two different service channels. Which interpretation is most appropriate?

A. Matching the mean is sufficient, so there is no concern.

B. The two peaks suggest the single Gamma distribution may not capture important structure in the observed data.

C. The response must be binary.

D. The model is invalid because Gamma random variables cannot be positive.

Answer 2.43

Click here to reveal the answer!

Correct answer: B.

Rationale:

A fitted distribution can match a mean while failing to capture important shape features. Two peaks may indicate unmodelled heterogeneity, such as different service channels or subpopulations. This is a goodness-of-fit concern even if the fitted mean looks reasonable.

Question 2.44

Open-ended Question

A clinical analyst fits a Beta distribution (see Section E.7) to a biomarker proportion \(Y\in(0,1)\). The observed data include many values close to \(0\) and many values close to \(1\), with few observations near the middle. Explain why checking only the sample mean could be misleading. Also, suggest a visual check and a numerical summary that would help assess fit.

Answer 2.44

Click here to reveal the answer!

Answer:

The sample mean can look reasonable even when the distributional shape is poorly captured. For example, a U-shaped distribution with many values near \(0\) and \(1\) can have a mean near \(0.50\), just like a distribution concentrated around \(0.50\). A useful visual check is a histogram or density plot of the observed proportions with the fitted Beta density overlaid. A useful numerical summary is a comparison of observed and fitted proportions in bins near \(0\), middle values, and near \(1\).
